The probe should be straight, without a bend, with a sharp point and the length of its blades equal. Be careful not to cut all or part of the skin when cutting, as it may cause inflammation at the site.
Cut it [the lesion] flat, if you are not able to grasp it with your forceps, then attract it with a hook and cut it. If three days have passed and it swells and becomes hot beneath the edge of the incision, leave it until the warm inflammation subsides and then cauterize it.
Cut it according to what suits you, and take care not to cut anything from the edge of the incision, for that does no harm. Treat it with whatever remedies we have described in our treatise on sores.
If you cut more than the necessary amount from the skin and draw it up, that too is not harmful to a great extent. Treat it as we have described until it heals.
The eighth and fifty-ninth chapter.
On the treatment of obstructed urine in the bladder.
Obstructed urine in the bladder is due to obstruction by stone, blood clot, pus or meat-like matter and similar things. If you treat it with what we have described for the side effects of treatment as mentioned in the classification.
If urine does not flow and you suspect that it is obstructed by a stone in the neck of the bladder, the patient should lie on his back, then a man should stand over him and press his abdomen against his chest while the patient holds himself.
When the stone is pressed at the neck of the bladder, it causes the stone to be expelled backwards and urine will flow. If it does not flow as described earlier and the condition worsens on the patient, extraction should be done with the instrument called Katharyr, here is its image:.
It is made from silver and is slender, shaped like a bird's beak in the end of a long tube, about the length of a span and a half. It has a soft handle at its head, and a drawing-up function for urine using threads, with a string or cord tightly tied to one end.
The end of the thread is inserted into the Katharyr and it is pulled gently if something comes out from the cord, so that it can enter the tube, then the Katharyr is lubricated with oil, ointment or egg white and the patient sits on a chair.
The bladder is uncovered and lubricated with wet cloth, oil or warm water, then the Katharyr is inserted into the uncovered part until it reaches the base of the instrument, then the instrument is pushed gently towards the inside.
Until the probe is completely inserted, push the forceps inside until it approaches the seat closely. The man inclines it downward and the forceps are within him, then push it until it reaches the stomach and he feels something empty.
The probe is positioned in this way because the channel through which urine flows has a bend, then the cord is threaded gently with some force, as urine follows the cord; it is then extracted and the urine released, and the forceps are returned.
Continue doing that until the stomach empties and the patient feels relieved.

Chapter Six.
On removing stones.
We have previously mentioned in the Anatomy section the types of stones and their treatment with drugs, and we distinguished between kidney stones and those in the bladder, as well as signs for identifying them. We also mentioned that some stones require surgical removal with fingers and forceps.
This is a stone produced specifically in the bladder, which appears as gravel and I shall describe it briefly and succinctly.
I say that the stone produced in the bladder is more likely to be subject to calculi, and among its signs is that urine comes out from the bladder resembling water in clumps.
It appears as gravel and he describes it well, he mentions that it is often accompanied by sand and frequently causes irritation. However, it can persist for up to ten years and becomes difficult in old age. For young people, it presents a moderate difficulty between the two extremes.
The one whose stone is larger has an easier treatment, and the smaller ones require more, so if we approach treatment, it is necessary to first drain the cause by draining all the pus that may be present in it as it may prevent detection of the stone during examination.
Then take the cause by its root and shake it downwards to let the stone fall into the bladder or push it from a higher place several times, then sit it between your hands with its head downward, hold it with your hand so that the bladder becomes all hollow towards the bottom, then examine it.
Examine it from outside, if you feel the stone in the cavity, then press it firmly, if it does not sink, tie a ligature to hold the bladder in place, then wipe the tip of the index finger of the left hand or the middle finger with oil if the cause is young or the index finger.
If it is a young one, place it in its position and examine the stone until it sinks under your finger, then move it gently towards the neck of the bladder, compress it with your finger and push it out in the direction you wish to crush it.
The attendant is ordered to press the bladder with his hand, and another attendant is ordered to push the testicles with his left hand towards above, while with his other hand he pushes the skin which is below the testicles on the side away from the location where the rupture will be. Then take the probe, as shown in this figure.
Crush the area between the bladder and the testicles not in the middle but on the left side of the scrotum, the rupture will be in the same bladder stone, grip it with your fingers and press it outwards so that the rupture is outside and wider from inside and tight on the bladder stone to the extent that the bladder stone can come out.
Do not press the finger too hard on the bladder stone during rupture as it may come out without difficulty, and know that some stones have edges and letters which make their removal difficult.
It is from this type of stone that looks like a ball and mobile, making its removal easy as it has no edges or letters. If not, increase the pressure slightly in rupture but if it does not come out in this way, it is necessary to compress it firmly with a flat forceps whose tip resembles a cold iron.
To press on the bladder so that it does not leak, or if you must enter beneath it with a delicate tool having a blunt tip. If you are unable to do so, widen the opening slightly. If something of blood emerges, cut it off with scissors.
If there are more than one, press the larger one towards the bladder first, then widen the opening on it. After that, remove the smaller one. Do this if there are more than two. If it is very large, be cautious as it may not be possible to widen the opening greatly because it could expose one of two risks.
Either it may lead to death or it may cause permanent urinary blockage because it cannot be moved from its position. Try to press and move it until it is expelled or it changes into a breakable state using forceps, until it is expelled in pieces.
Once you have completed your procedure, seal the wound with a candle and wait for it to cool. Then apply warm water or oil and wrap it with linen cloth, and let the heat remain until the worm dies. After that, apply pressure on the affected area and do not undo the bandage until the third day.
If a wound or ulcer swells, it should be washed with much water and oil. Then treat it with al-murham al-nukhuliy and al-murham al-basliqun until it abscesses. If it appears in the incision or abscess that is hot, inflamed, suppurating, and similar such as blood congealing in the bladder and preventing urination, this can be known by the exit of blood along with urine.
Place your finger in the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains, it leads to the corruption of the bladder and its destruction. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat it with whatever remedy is suitable until it abscesses.
It is recommended during all treatment periods to tie the veins and gather them together to keep the medicine that is applied on the area, for if the abscess is small and has settled in the urethra and blocked it preventing urination, treat it as I have described before it becomes ulcerated.
Often I have found this treatment effective against ulceration. It is to take a piece of steel wool and shape it into the form shown here:.
Like a right angled triangle with a sharp point on the base, then take a string and tie it around the urethra below the abscess so that it does not return to the bladder. Then insert the steel wool into the abscess until it reaches the abscess itself.
Apply the probe gently into the bladder with your hand, move it around little by little and you will feel a puncture. Then hold onto what remains of the bladder outside the scrotum, for it will shatter and come out along with the urine, and the pain will subside.
If this treatment does not work for you due to a blockage preventing it, tie a string under the bladder and another one above the bladder. Then squeeze the bladder within the scrotum between the strings and it will come out.
Then untie the string and clean the clotted blood that has formed in the wound. The string must be tied under the bladder so it does not return to the bladder, and the other one from above to ensure that when the string is untied after the bladder comes out, the skin will return to its place covering the wound.
To achieve this, if you tie the upper string, raise the skin above so it returns to its place when you release it and covers the wound as we said.
Chapter One and Two.
On removing the bladder in women.
Bladder stones are rarely found in women. If one is presented to you, it makes treating her difficult and many will refuse to allow a woman to be examined because she might not be a virgin, or they will not find a woman who allows herself to be treated.
If it is slim or from the forbidden women, or if you do not find a woman skilled in this craft, especially not to work with her hands and feet, the fourth point is that the fracture on the bone should be distant from the joint, thus requiring a different incision.
In this case, it is dangerous, so if necessity requires that, then it is necessary to take a competent female doctor or if she cannot be found, seek an expert male doctor, or bring a woman who is skilled in women's matters, or a woman who has some experience in this craft.
Bring her and instruct her to examine the bone first, as she should check if the woman is menstruating. In that case, it is necessary for her to insert her finger into the vagina and examine the bone. If she finds it and presses it under her fingernail.
Then instruct her to make the incision, as she should not be menstruating and if she is pregnant, then allow one who is skilled to insert her finger into the armpit and feel for the bone with her left hand while firmly rotating it.
If you find it (the tumor), it is necessary to lower it from the mouth of the stomach downwards by its depth until it reaches the bottom of the intestine, then break it open near the midpoint of the abdomen, at the root of the intestine, whichever side it comes from. Feel for the stone in that direction.
Press it (the tumor) to ensure that the stone is not pressed underneath, and make the opening small at first. Then introduce a tool into this small opening. If you feel for the stone, increase the opening as much as you know that the stone will come out. Know that there are various types of stones.
Among them are small and large, smooth and rough, long and rounded, and with hair. Know their types to determine what you want, for if blood overwhelms you, apply a coagulant powder in the area and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then return to your work.
Until the stone comes out, work with the tools mentioned in the male's extraction of stones, using them to assist you in your work. If bleeding overwhelms you and you realize it is dangerous, apply cautery to the area.
"After it has become firmly knit, leave it and do not try to open it again, and do not remove the suture. Perhaps the wound will heal. If a pus accumulates after days and the place relaxes, return to your work until the abscess is expelled.
"Second and sixth paragraph
"On the abscess on the water bladder.
"The water bladder is a collection of fluid under the tough white sac, which surrounds the testicle and is covered by a membrane called the scrotum, and it can be found in a specific location within the testicle.
"It should be noted that it is not another egg, but rather it is between the scrotum and the white sac we mentioned earlier, and this abscess does not occur except in the testicle. This abscess occurs due to a weakness that is susceptible to women, so this substance accumulates there after an injury to the testicle.
"This fluid may have various colors, either yellow, blood-red, brown, black, or white, and the signs that indicate it.
If water gathers, it is to be noted that if [it] is in the whitish scrotum (we have said earlier) the worm will grow little in length, resembling an egg, and its characteristic feature will not show because moisture surrounds it from all sides.
If moisture is in the scrotum (the whitish one) mentioned earlier, then the worm will grow little by little resembling an egg.
If a man supposes that it is another white egg, and moisture is between the scrotum and the whitish scrotum, then it occurs under the skin. As for if you want to know the color of the moisture, squeeze the worm with a warm dye which you have prepared and shown its image; whatever does not come out from the dye, judge it by what is inside it.
When treating with iron, it is necessary that the patient be ordered to withdraw if possible and you see his body trembling, then he lies on something slightly elevated and a large number of cuts are made beneath him, then you sit on his left side.
Order the servant to sit on his right side, lead him to one side of the scrotum and to the side of the abdomen, then take a wide-mouthed container and make an incision in the middle of the scrotum nearly up to the pain.
The incision should be made evenly parallel to the line that divides the skin of the scrotum in half until it reaches the white cord containing it, then it is cut and preserved from being further incised, and the cut should be made more on the side adjoining the testicle. The incision is then made as shallow as possible.
Next, fill the cord with water and widen it as much as possible and let all the water out, then separate the edges of the incision with scissors and raise the cord above and do not touch the surrounding skin, and cut the cord as much as you can, either in one piece or cutting it piece by piece.
Be careful not to cut the thin side, for if you do not make a shallow incision, the water will not escape. If the testicle protrudes from its skin during your procedure, stop cutting the cord and return it to its place, then sew up the skin of the scrotum.
Next, treat all other types of surgeries until the patient recovers. If the testicle is infected with a previous disease, it is necessary to tie off the vessels that fear bleeding, then cut the cord and remove the testicle, and treat it as previously described.
If water accumulates in both cavities, know that it is a double hernia. Break the other cavity with what was done in the first one, regardless of its position, even if you think the operation is one. Then, smear blood.
Enter the hernias with threads dipped in oil or tallow, let another thread come out that has been dipped in alcohol and vinegar, spread it on the hernia and the abdominal cavity, place a stuffed sack above the crack (refracture), and these are the remedies. Tie them from above with a ligature that has six sides in this shape:.

Chapter Eighty Seven.
On the extraction of the cataract.
If the cataract is held after breathing, it is necessary to order the assistant to grasp her (the patient) and then to squeeze it with forceps and press your hand on her mouth and pull it out with this, otherwise grab a tool.
And drill a hole in the seal and place in it the open-mouthed forceps such as fudanj, sadab, shabt, babunj, silyakh, qantourioun, all of these or some of them, immerse them in water and bring them to fire then place a red tube on the hole of the seal and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
Hold it until the liquid reaches the abdominal cavity, then let it go as we have said before, for the pus will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, command the one who can do so to insert his left hand in warm oil or unguent.
Then insert it into the wound and search for the pus, if you find it grasp it gently and pull it out little by little until it comes out. If it is deeply attached in the abdominal cavity, insert your hand as we have described, then when you find the pus, attract it gently little by little towards the surface.
To prevent the abdomen from falling during strong pulling, it is advisable to move it gently towards both sides (right and left) and then gradually increase the amount of pulling. For when it responds in this manner, it will return and dislodge itself from the attachment. If there is a fistula in the abdomen, we have described the treatment as letting go and applying pressure and using cautery.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, then exert force on it again in pulling, but it is advisable to tie what comes out from it to the vagina, then cut it with a four-edged knife (cautery), for it will heal after several days.
If it softens and comes out, it shall only be so unless it hardens again; for then it will emit a foul smell towards the affected part and the head, causing pain. Therefore, it is necessary to use agreeable smoke.
Some ancient women have experimented with the smoke of herbs such as poppy and castor oil, an image of the machine which a woman uses to inhale this smoke during childbirth and similar situations:.
It is made of brass or iron, the thin end is placed in the opening and the wide end is heated with charcoal and the smoke is trapped inside until it goes out and a fresh one comes in.
The ninth chapter and seventy.
On the treatment of non-pierced hemorrhoids.
If many children are born and their hemorrhoids are not pierced, it can be treated by a thin needle; if the patient is unable to do this herself, she should tie it with a sharp cord while being careful not to touch the nerves.
Then, a wick soaked in alcohol and oil is placed on it, and it is treated with cautery until it scabs over. If one fears that it may break open again, a thin tube of brass can be inserted into the piercing for several days until the child wants to pass stool.
The contusion (bruise) of the sciatic nerve may also occur from injury or disease. It is necessary to dissipate that condition, then treat it as previously described.
The Chapter is Eighteen.
On the treatment of the veins that occur in the lower parts.
The veins that occur in the lower parts are strangled and swollen. This happens near the sciatic nerve from outside or within one of its sides, and the vein(s) may be single or multiple.
If that strangulation occurs, it is necessary to open it and allow fluid - white or darkish yellow - to flow out. This may be one of the veins leading to the anus (hemorrhoid) or the bladder, or not leading to any of those. The ones that do lead out are known for the pus and foul air released upon applying the cautery.
Or there may be no pus, and it may be one of the veins if it is leading to the bladder or the urethra (male urinary duct), or leading to the pubic bone or the penis, and what is known as a vein leading to the sciatic nerve is also included in this category without leading to the sciatic nerve itself.
"Insert your little finger into the nostril, then insert a thin probe made of copper or iron if there is no splinter in the nostril. If there is a splinter, insert a thin probe made of lead or hair from the horse's mane.
"Feel for the splinter or hair with your little finger. If you do not feel anything protruding from the wound, nor smell or pus as we have previously mentioned, then know that it is not penetrated and proceed to treatment.
"If the splinter has penetrated to the throat or the urinary tract, a sign is the passage of urine from it and its refusal to be treated with medication. As for when it has penetrated to the palm of the hand or to the wrist, a sign is the arrival of the probe at that location.
"If there is no bone splinter and the pain is in that area, with continuous bleeding from it and no treatment or medication helping, these are all cases where there is no protrusion of the splinter.
"The treatment of such cases is ineffective for those who ignorantly try to treat them, and as for the ones which are not penetrated or fixed, let it be cauterized with iron as I have described and as I have tried, by pressing the affected area between your hands on its surface.
The excerpt translates to: "He should bring out the needle upward, and insert his hand into the abdomen. Then, a copper or lead wire is to be introduced if it is in the nasal cavity, until he learns where the wire ends. If it feels like a seated object, then it is necessary for you to insert your little finger at the seated point.
"If you feel the wire in your little finger, it means that it has emerged by itself without pain, except if you feel a roughness or flesh between your finger and it. In this case, be certain that it has emerged. Do not tire yourself trying to pull it out if there is no pain as we said earlier.
"It is said that it may cause suffocation in some people when the nasal cavity is blocked. The treatment that can be beneficial for this is to apply a thin tube made of a material suitable for the size of the nostril, such as silk or linen, and insert it into the nostril until it reaches the seated point, then withdraw it twice or thrice.
"Until you are certain that all the excess and swollen tissues, resembling the feathers of a bird's quill, have burned. Then, treat it with an ointment made of oil and resin until the burnt tissues come out. Afterwards, apply a salve to heal it.
Unless he can extract it without causing more harm, a foreign body should not be left in the wound forever. If a splinter has entered into the bone that is seated, there will be thick tissue, either meat or cartilage, between them. If you see a pustule on the surface of the skin next to the splinter, break it open when the skin around the pustule first appears. Follow the incision with the splinter, which is in the pustule, until you reach the edge of the splinter where it exits and the splinter comes out and falls off. Then clean the congealed flesh that resembles bird feathers, leaving nothing behind.
Then treat the wound with caustic soda until it is healed. If bleeding persists and there is a gap between you and your work, cut off the excessive flesh. It is better to do so with fire or a sharp instrument, as boiling kills the excess flesh.
Cut the blood and remove the moisture, then treat with infused oil of roses or powdered charcoal in oil until the situation is resolved and the burned flesh comes out in the discharge. Then treat the wound immediately with hot cauterizing iron for tough meat.
The medication which contains a binding agent and cleans is such that if it swells, it has been purged and there will be no danger of recurrence. If the Naṣūr has reached deep into the wound and beyond the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the wound and explore with it.
If you feel a cord and there is a covering of membrane or flesh between you and it, and it is close to the opening, then work should not be done on it except by the method of hope and prayer. This is because one may use one of three approaches: either to tie it as we have mentioned before.
Either it may be untied, until it reaches its hole, so that a probe can be inserted for treatment close by, without causing the surrounding nerves to be cut, thus preventing the boil from bursting unintentionally. Then treat it as we have mentioned before, but there is a possibility of purge as we said earlier.
Either it may be removed and cut, after untying and drawing it near the wound as we have mentioned before, either by thread or with another sharp tool. Then clean all the pus-filled flesh in the wound until you can remove it by cutting.
Then, make an incision in the wound completely with the blade and the upper jaw until it is closed. The nasal opening will remain inside the socket. This method will be less painful for the patient, and the nose may be deformed on this characteristic.
If you insert the probe into the nose and it is next to the socket on the side of the body with skin and the edge of the socket, take a probe when this happens, pierced at its end like an awl in this manner:.
Place a thread loosely through five or more strands and then insert the probe into the nose with the thread until it reaches the cavity, if it is open in the socket from within close by.
Pull the thread from that hole by inserting your finger into the socket and pulling the end of the thread out, gathering both ends together and tightening them, then leave it for a day or two until the string cuts through the flesh between the threads very firmly, causing those pieces of flesh to fall off. Then treat the wound until it heals and scabs over.
If the upper jaw is not open, insert it as far as possible without risk of injury to nerves, then proceed as previously described until it heals and scabs over.

Chapter One and Twenty-Eight.
On the incision of the veins, which exudes blood and cutting it and treating the cracks.
Veins are on the arteries, either they are inside a cavity resembling red ants or like grapes, and there are small and large ones, and blood is always exuding from them.
One is present, and many are, and they are outside the cavity, except that those which are outside the cavity have less moisture and exude a small amount of white water or little blood constantly, and their color is the same as the body.
The treatment for what is inside the cavity is to order the patient to stretch and sweat until the cavity appears, and then you will see the clots, hurry up and hold them with forceps or grasp them with your fingers, then cut it at its roots, if it does not adhere to the forceps due to its moisture and softness.
Apply a thick splint or a piece of firm bandage to it, or draw it with your fingers, then cut it, and afterward rub on it with some pointed ointments so that its place may heal quickly or let it dry on the matter described in the chapter of sutures.
Treat it with all kinds of treatment until it heals, if the abscess does not require lancing, inject oil into it to clean what is in the abscess and hasten its discharge as soon as pus appears, for abscesses that are outside the abscess should be treated with hot water.
Take it with tweezers or tie it with a strong thread, then cut it and treat it as we have mentioned until it heals. If there is aversion to cutting with iron, use forceps of this type; take a loose thread and insert it into the needle.
Draw the abscess up to its source from the other side and insert the needle into it, then pull the ends of the thread below the needle tightly, the abscess will be resistant and tighten strongly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this for all abscesses and leave one untreated.
Draw out the pus from it (the wound), then place on the cushion a linen or silk bag filled with oil and put it on the wound, and command the patient to be quiet, and leave him until the pus drops out. If the pus falls out, treat it with leeches and other treatments until it heals.
The treatment for ulcers is often due to accumulation of phlegm and obstruction, if it persists and the medicine does not help, then it should be scratched with a lancet or a blunt instrument until it becomes moist and the upper layer that prevents healing peels off.
Then treat it until it heals as required, if it does not heal, repeat the procedure with a stronger scarification until it bleeds and then treat it, for it will heal.
Chapter Two and Eighty.
On the treatment of twisted fractures, non-twisted fractures and dry pus and ulcers.
The fracture is a continuous band on the body resembling a camel's hump that occurs throughout the body, especially in the lower limbs and fingers, and it causes pain during walking.
It is necessary to cut what surrounds the sutures, and hold with forceps or scissors, then remove its base, and treat it. As for the hardened thorns, one of them is the small, hard, pointed nettles found on the surface of the body.
Often found on the hands of children is the thorn, and its treatment is to apply warm oil or wool thread or hair until it falls off, or you can burn it with fire or medicine that dissolves it.
The opposite and non-opposite thorns can be removed with the machine I mentioned in the treatment of scorpions. As for scorpions, they are also small pointed nettles that are very deep within the body and when it freezes, its pain is similar to a wasp sting.
There is a worm in the body, and it is most commonly found in the hands. Its treatment is to take a tube from an eagle feather or hawk quill and make that tube surround the worm so that its circular circumference encompasses it. Then, rotate your hand around the tube until what surrounds the worm is cut.
It will sink into the flesh and the maggot will be removed from its source, if you wish to do so, you can do it with a copper or iron tube on this image:.
It should be higher at the tip to be easily grasped and squeezed, and if you wish, you can put the maggot in any of the tubes found at the opening:.
The Third and Twenty-Eighth Section.
In the images of tools that are used for puncturing in cases of hemorrhoids, fistulas and ulcers (abscesses).
The needle can be made of gold, silver, copper or brass, and this tool can be made in various sizes according to the users. The tool used for treating the minor wounds of infants and those with narrow or painful buttocks should be small, whereas the one used for larger wounds is larger, image of a large needle:.
The upper part that binds the ligature should be wide for it to have a grip where the ligature can be tightened as you see, and the lower part which enters into the buttock should be narrow towards the tip on one side with two holes and on the other side with one hole.
The cavity of the wound should be on the white part of the bone, or slightly on the yellow. The pouch that you make with the drugs from an animal bladder or a thin bag made of skin, should be small in size, being like a small pouch.
Drill numerous holes as much as possible and let them be on the white part of the bone, then put threads and needles made of a fine silk through each hole, and collect all the holes with the pouch (pouch-like structure) similar to a small sack. If the medicine is placed firmly at the tip of the lancet between the two bones, then prick with it. This image shows a circular pouch and hole arrangement.
The method of pricking is to fill the bladder or the bag with medicine as much as the likelihood allows, and be careful not for it to become too hot or too cold. Then the patient is made to lie on his back, and the pricker raises the patient's arm above his head.
Place a cloth or mat under him, then smear the handle of the lancet with oil or white lead or something similar, then insert it into the pouch and place it in the holder. Then the attendant takes hold of the pouch with his hands and shakes it violently until everything inside the lancet is expelled from its tip.
Then, the lancet is drawn out and the opening is filled with the remedy for expulsion; if the medicine adheres to it, it should be gripped firmly and even if he sleeps all through the night, it will have effect; if a lancet is injected into an ulcer of the eye and hastily withdrawn, let the lancet be held on until it is released.
If there is a boil or painful abscess in the seat, let the lancet be very soft and thin, milder than those used for children's lancing; this is an image of a soft, tight-held lancet:.
The Fourth and the Eighty-Seventh Chapter.
On Surgical Treatments.
I have previously mentioned in the classification of diseases what requires excision and now I shall explain their treatment by hands and medicines to perfection; I say that surgeries differ according to the thing which is subjected to the incision and according to the place where the cut occurs.
The things subjected to incision are numerous, such as stone, metal objects, swords or knives, bones, and other similar objects.
And regarding the incisions according to body parts, they are such as an incision on the head, neck, chest, abdomen or back and similar organs.
I will describe the treatment of some surgeries to make them a standard, and my rules apply to all other surgeries. I shall begin with simple head surgeries as compound ones will be discussed in the third chapter of this book.
Whenever a simple wound occurs in the head and it is not a bone fracture, if it appears to be due to a stone or similar object and only skin has been torn, and the wound is large and one fears the occurrence of a severe burn, I advise to cauterize with heat at the site. Do not delay this and extract from the wound an amount of blood to its extent of strength, unless prevented by something. Also, it is better that the blood which comes out of the wound flows towards the injured side.
Warn the patient against excessive drinking, eating rich food and alcohol, as they may prevent cauterization. In such a case, he should eat less and avoid meat and drink, and apply to the wound if burn occurs with a hot fluid or one containing alcohol.
If the burn is not severe, apply to the wound with its own blood and let air reach it for a little while as I have described to you earlier, but if the air has changed it, apply some ointment.
Until the wound drains, then treat it until it heals. If the injury is severe and caused by a sword or similar and the edges do not meet, sew it together with sutures as described in the abdominal surgeon's stitching.
If the skin is torn from bone and there is no tissue adhering to it except in a loose area, cut it, then treat the wound with medicines that make the flesh tough like oak bark and command the patient to eat nutritious foods such as heads (of animals) and garlic, etc.
If a blister occurs in the skin and does not adhere to the bone, cut all of it and then treat it. In these kinds of wounds there may be veins or arteries that bleed profusely and are not stopped by medicines. In such cases, probe the veins.
If the wound is not deep, tie it with a ligature and if necessary, if what we have mentioned does not help, wait until the bleeding stops. These kinds of wounds are simple and easy to treat; all that is required is to let them heal by themselves with their own blood.
Before it is affected by air, the recipe for the plaster of emeralds consists of taking a portion from milk, a portion from eggs and three portions from either cooked or uncooked lime. All are crushed and mixed together to treat wounds, making them strong and sticky so they adhere well to the injury and harden strongly.
It is to be sewn up with sutures, then left for the pus to drain from above without being allowed to heal until it does not cause swelling anymore; because the medicine will not work on it otherwise. Lime can do this alone if milk and eggs are not available.
This action can also be done with both sides in many large wounds, if it is mastered and placed on it and sutured, and the wounds were clean and not affected by air, whereas if they were affected by air partially or especially during summer.
To apply this plaster, it means treating it with what we have mentioned before, i.e., placing some tied clamps on it or applying a poultice made of fine barley flour and water and honey until the pus is drained, then treating it with other treatments until it heals.
If a fracture occurs along with a wound in the bone that is simple, draw it together with forceps. I have mentioned the treatment of skull fractures in an earlier section, in the surgery of the neck. If a wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in the treatment between this and a simple skull fracture.
If a vein or artery has been cut from the neck, if it is an artery, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with strong constricting devices such as forceps, scissors and similar instruments because they harm the artery and cause it to contract.
Do not place anything cold on the wound because the surface of the artery is cold and its connection with the brain, which is the noblest of organs, can be treated with mild medicines such as cleaned olive oil or melted butter and warm water.
If you wash the wound and all metal stones and damp bandages with warm water, and if the wound is large, use stitching or tie it up with string then treat it until it heals.
If there is a cavity in the wound and pus has collected at the bottom, squeeze it out to a lower place, if an artery has been cut in the wound and its blood spurts, tie it or cauterize it if necessary.
If the wound has severed some hairs of the scalp or all of it and the edges have been sutured, gather the skin together by sewing on a ball of the scalp and do not press the scalp but rather smooth and spread it out in its natural shape, then tighten it firmly and leave it for days.
Then treat it with antimony and drying until it forms a crust. If the wound has exposed bone, examine it to see if there are fragments of bone. If so, remove all or as much as possible that cannot be removed and leave it until the wound heals and forms a scab.
If the wound is ulcerated, let it dry on its own. If bone necrosis occurs and turns black, treat as described previously, for thoracic and intercostal surgery.
If it is a wound caused by sword or knife, and you see a cavity in it, check if air escapes when the patient breathes, in which case it is a war wound. If there is no cavity and it is ulcerated with blood, do not apply anything to it at first and do not tighten it lest the blood clots in its cavity.
Put it into the heart, and you will kill the ailment, but make in it two cauterizing irons and heat them in the daytime twice. If it does not appear for you, make in the wound of the injured person a bladder with vinegar to absorb what comes out of it from the moisture, and put the patient to sleep on the injury.
Cure what accumulates in it, and if it has passed more than three days for the injury without the ailment manifesting or any swelling, or constriction of the throat or tightness in the soul, and you see all its conditions good, then know that the wound is safe; treat it with ointment and other remedies until it heals.
If it becomes hard to probe and the fish remains constant, then know that it has become a scab; treat it at its opening, let the ailment be cured in the wound, whether it is on the chest or back; treat it with whatever stitching comes first.
If it is large or has bumps, whether it is large or small; if it has affected the bone and cut pieces from it, probe the wound and try to extract those pieces if they are free; if they are not free, leave them until.
The incision (wound) is easy to treat, as for other surgeries that occur in various body parts, their treatment is as we have previously mentioned.
The Fifth and Eighteenth Section.
On abdominal surgery, removal of the foreign body, and its suturing.
A wound that occurs in the abdomen may be large or small and may be central, in which case it may expel a foreign body or multiple foreign bodies. The process of introducing them and suturing is more difficult and challenging.
A small wound may also be difficult from another perspective, requiring the return to be more challenging and the removal of the foreign body to be difficult if it does not enter at the right time, causing swelling and making its insertion difficult for this reason, a medium-sized wound is considered better because it does not cause additional difficulty with the return of the foreign body, as it is in these two types.
And know that abdominal suturing can be done in two ways, one of which is suitable for abdominal suturing and the other for suturing other abdominal surgeries, while the others are specific to other surgeries except that they specialize in abdominal suturing.
And I shall explain to you one by one, with detail and clarity. If the wound is small and something from the vein comes out of it and it tightens up, that is either because the wound is shallow, as we have mentioned, or because the vein was exposed to breath inhalation.
If it is like that, it is necessary to cauterize it by touching a damp sponge or lint in boiling water and then apply it to the vein until it coagulates and stops bleeding.
The drink that contains a clot is better than water in stopping bleeding, so if you see the bleeding has stopped, press the vein with boiled water using tweezers or cloth, because it will make its return easier.
If its return is difficult after this treatment, make a small incision in the wound with an instrument that cuts veins and perform the initial treatment again if possible, because it is better than making an incision and only do so when absolutely necessary and when other methods fail, as shown in this image:.
The narrow end of the instrument should be curved and the other end should not be curved, and the tip should not be sharp but slightly rounded, and it is an instrument resembling a small scoop as you can see. If the wound expands and the vein enters it, its return should be in its natural form.
If possible, the surgeon should place himself in a position that is best for him. However, if the wound is extensive and located in the lower abdomen, he should make the patient lie on his back and raise the affected side above his head. If it is in the upper abdomen, he should raise the patient's head and chest from below.
If the wound is in one of the two sides of the abdomen, make your intention and aim always to raise the side where the surgery is taking place above the other side. This should be done during major surgeries and during middle surgeries as well.
In small surgeries, place the patient as you deem fit, then gather an assistant by your side who will hold the wound with both hands and press it together. Then, let a person in charge of sewing open a little at a time for sewing.
This is the standard method of suturing, where you take a thread or multiple threads according to the size of the wound, then leave a margin of skin around the wound before stitching with only one needle without inserting any thread into it. With the scalpel that is beneath the skin, from inside to bring it out from that side, and my incisions of the skin and the scalpel became four squares, then I tighten a twisted thread around the vein in both directions until the wound edges meet firmly.
Then, leave the fingernail's side open and tie another vein, then bind it with thread as you did with the first vein, continue to do so for any additional required veins until all the wound is drained and the vein is moderately situated between the bone and the flesh.
Because the cutting vein is very sharp and quickly cuts meat and bone as well, making it difficult to enter the skin, therefore, it is best that the vein be situated in the middle of the bone and flesh, and not tied near the edge of the skin.
To prevent the meat from being cut too quickly and to avoid delaying the suturing, do not tie it too far away, then cut the edges of the vein so as not to harm the patient while sleeping and to make benefits from applying cataplasms on both sides of the vein.
Leave it until you learn that incision is healed, and this kind of suturing with catgut is more suitable for minor surgeries because it may be sufficient in its suturing with a single or two catguts or similar.
As for the second technique, it is to gather four sutures which mean the skin and subcutaneous tissue stitches in one go with catgut having a thread well balanced in thickness and tension. Then when this catgut is used to sew these four sutures, draw the catgut from the side where it started.
The thread should be knotted from the top of the incision so that the suturing is done according to the stitching of the akinetosis which causes less pain and makes between each stitch and the next by pressing the small finger this kind of suturing is known to all people.
With this suturing, a surgeon can perform an incision exposed on the abdomen of a man who was stabbed with two knives and the wound was larger than a finger and had exited from his waist about 15 fingers from the central navel and the wound was in the middle of the abdomen.
He recovered after keeping his fracture outside the wound for four and twenty hours, then the wound started healing in about fifteen days, and he was treated until he purged, and afterwards lived many years, managing all his affairs. The physicians ruled that he would not be cured of the fracture (gout).
It is surprising that I did not treat him with medicine because I was in a place where there was nothing to find for medication, so I put on the wound lint twice a day and committed to washing it with honey water until he purged.
And as for one of the specific types of embroidery, I have mentioned it on the word of Galenus, that it begins with the skin and enters from outside to inside; if the thread comes out in the skin and in the sinew that runs along the length of the backbone, all of it.
I removed the stitch from the side where I inserted the thread, and the thread came out on the other side of the embroidery from inside to outside in the other side; if it comes out, take another one in the same place from outside to inside.
Open the scissors on one side and pierce it with a needle in its other side from inside to outside, and pierce together with your puncture into the scissor until you pierce all of them into the handle which is on that side.
Then, also start from this side with his eye and trace it along the handle of the scissor which is on the other side, and draw out the needle from the skin near it, then return it to that skin and trace the handle of the scissor on the other side with this handle of the thread.
Draw it out from the skin which is on that side, and do this again until you stitch all the incision with this kind of stitching. This type of stitching is called the stitches for fraying [fabric].
Stitch the scissor once from one side with the skin and leave it on the other side until it is empty, and as for the second type of unique stitching that he also mentioned [Al-Zahrawi], this is his word.
Stitch it in a way that a group of healers stitches all its parts to a matching problem for him, by connecting the handles of the scissor together and the handles of the thread together, and this will be what is suitable for him.
It is necessary to tie the suture outside and insert it into [the wound] by itself, then leave the suturing needle aside, then pull the suture out from outside to inside in the same place, then insert it again outside to inside in the same location. Do this twice, then pull it out again.
Pull the suture from inside to outside at another site that is opposite [the first one], and this stitch is better than a simple, easy stitch which he mentioned earlier. This statement is by Galenus in his writing, and some say that abdominal suturing with two sutures may be crooked. Insert a single thread and start by inserting one suture from your side and pulling it to the other side, then insert the other suture from the opposite side of where you started, pull it from your side according to the pattern of knitting a sock.
Know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, then suturing it will be more difficult than other areas of the abdomen. As for sewing the wound, its judgement is like that of all wounds and that is: if you find the wound straight with the flesh before air or moisture changes it, and you have removed the dirt and marked it, then cover it with lint.
If the wound has been altered by air, apply some cauteries until it seizes and the threads fall out and the cautery burns the flesh and the muscle, then treat it as you would any other surgery until it heals.
If you do not have medication, apply a clean linen cloth on it from the start and change it twice a day as you know, until it heals. In most cases, there is no need for additional treatment if the surgeries are simple. However, if you fear that the main joints will share in the pain.
It is necessary to apply a linen cloth in warm olive oil or butter and place it over the areas between the armpits and groin, if you feel pain or swelling in these places, this may help greatly by drinking a concoction of strong black tea.
If the swelling has reached the point where there is an opening to the abdomen, know that whatever is thick in the armpit area will be easier to heal, while what is thin will be more difficult.
As for the known swelling, it does not accept healing from a surgery that involves the buttock, because there is a large amount of veins and bones there, as well as its proximity to the sciatic nerve. However, if you have encountered a wound that is shallow and you can reach it easily, then drain it according to your ability to drain the swelling.
If it has a duration and turns green or black, it should be pressed with thread above the darkened area so as not to expose blood flow. For in the wound there are veins and arteries, then cut what is beyond that band.
Make the edges of the thread protrude from below the surgery, outside it, to facilitate its tightening and removal upon falling of the stitching and healing of the wound. Remember the surgery that exposes a crack in the wound and is small.
It may happen that some people, because they saw a man who had been stabbed in his abdomen with a spear and the wound was on the right side of the navel, he (the doctor) treated it as if he did not dare to treat him (the patient). I did not cauterize it until it erupted and swelled at the site, then when I saw the swelling, I feared that something bad might happen to him in his depth. However, it did not develop a severe abscess from this, and he remained in good condition. He eats and drinks and gathers together and goes to the bathhouse and enjoys himself in his service, and some of those experienced have said that if he is exposed in the private part (genitals) and is small, it is necessary to circumcise him.
He takes large wasps, then he cuts off their heads. Then, he collects the stings and places a nest from them, with open mouths on the wounds. If he catches it (the wasp) and tightens it, it sticks and does not dissolve. Then, he places another nest near the first one.
And keep doing that with several wasps according to the wound size, then press it (the wound) and tie it up so that the heads remain sticking in the private part until the private part becomes inflamed and swollen, and no severe abscess happens, and you can also tie the private part with a thin thread.
The one who binds the wounded animal after it has entered an artery, should take a part of this thread from the animals, then tie a fine, flexible cord around it before inserting it into the artery. In it is a thread of the animals, wrapped with it is the wound before inserting into the depths (of the body). This beating of the threads with the snake and the animals is only on the path of recklessness and hope.
If the wound is large and wide, and without doubt if it is in one of the soft arteries, there is no trick (or method) for healing and no relief from death.
Sixteenth Chapter.
Treatment of hemorrhoids and piles.
Know that every wound or injury, if it persists and develops into a boil which does not heal, and if it constantly discharges pus (or matter), is called in general terms "piles" or "hemorrhoids", regardless of the limb where they occur. The Arabic term for them is "nāṣūr" and we call them "zakām.
It is true that a swollen white stone without pain has the shape of an egg like the beak of a bird.
Due to this, some of them call it a cyst, and at times it becomes wet permanently, causing putrefaction; however, the moisture may sometimes break in certain instances, and this moisture can be abundant or scanty, thick or thin.
Every abscess does not escape or grow in it, for there are several reasons, one of which is a lack of blood in the body or its blockage, or because within it and on its surface, there is hard muscle tissue that prevents good meat from growing. It may be abundant in pus and festering, or because the abscess itself is rotten and the substance that contains it has poor quality, or because the medicine is not suitable for treating it, or due to a malady that occurred in the town of Wabasha, or due to a specific problem in the town.
It may be abundant in pus and festering, or because the abscess itself is rotten and the substance that contains it has poor quality, or because the medicine is not suitable for treating it, or due to a malady that occurred in the town of Wabasha, or due to a specific problem in the town. As seen in the city of Saragossa, where it is difficult to diagnose and treat diseases; and perhaps because there is a single or multiple bone within it, and all these causes and their symptoms and treatments have been clearly demonstrated and explained with medicine in the textbook.
It is necessary for me to inform you about the treatment of nasal ulcers and scabs that are treated by hand manipulation. Know that veins can appear in all body parts, some of which end up in large arteries or nerves.
Some of them [veins] end up in the fingernail or wrist, or in the phalanx of the fingers on the hand and foot, and they approach closely to the joints such as the wrist joint and so forth.
Among these veins are those diseases that cannot be treated or manipulated easily, for if they are not at the locations mentioned and desired, then there is hope for their treatment.
If you wish to know that, then take a copper or iron probe if the nasal ulcer is firm, and test it with the probe. If there is a thickening in the nasal ulcer, use a sharp lead probe, as lead softens the body.
If the nasal ulcer has many cavities, you will not be able to probe it with a probe, so press one of its cavities and the moisture that is pressed from it will flow towards the other cavity and dissolve it.
From here, feel for any bone, muscle, or nerve nearby. Determine if the navus is deep or shallow, or if there are multiple navi present. Stand firm on all these points based on your ability to discern its presence and the pain that arises when you touch the location.
These are some of the indications and causes of pain, so if you stand firm on all these points and your conclusion is true, then look for treatment based on that and it is to observe if there is.
If the navus is visible nearby or in a safe location far from tendon, muscle, nerve, vein, or any of the locations mentioned for you, then break the navus according to the description provided and remove what is inside it of decaying flesh, rotten meat, and excessive meat.
If no growth is found in the mouth, or anything similar to that, and it is treated until it heals, if the navus is deep-seated and away from the edge and standing firm, then break it deeply as much as you can, then clean it completely of all decaying meat.
Then use the suppurating wound for sharp medicines and grind it into the nasal cavity which is reached with iron, do that repeatedly until the sharp medicine consumes all the corruption left in the nasal cavity.
Then force him to vomit with emetics that cause meat to come up correctly, if he does not vomit with this, press on what precedes and describe it, if the cause of the nasal cavity problem is bone and it is sound according to you, probe it and break it at the point where you were informed that it does not prevent you from.
If a nerve, vein or limb of the head is exposed as we mentioned earlier and if you find bone inside it with some corruption and blackness, expel it until the corruption goes away, then treat it with what heals it until it heals and comes back to normal, if it does not heal and the wound worsens as it used to.
Know that you have not reached all the corruptions, so expose everything surrounding it and cut away its membrane and clean it with great effort, then force him to vomit. If he vomits, it is good, otherwise know that this corruption is in a deep cavity far from the main corruption, so expose everything surrounding it again.
Extract the pus and clean it with great effort, for the decayed bone is small and can be drawn out. Draw it out with fine forceps that are suitable for this purpose.
If there are many bones, extract and draw them all carefully without leaving anything behind. If something from the ligaments resists you, but there is no risk in it, cut those ligaments and flesh if present, then clean the bone.
If you are unable to extract the bone at your convenience, bear the wound and leave it for a few days until the flesh and ligaments around the bone are softened. Keep the wound clean so that it does not become infected as a result of the incision you made while working on it.
Place a dressing over it if you are afraid of that, made from cotton soaked in pulverized quicklime and oil or a cotton dressing soaked in Egyptian natron or any other green antiseptic. For when it softens the ligaments and exposes the bone, it will be easy to extract and draw it out.
If there is a single large bone like the shin or thigh bone, and only part of it is decayed, cleanly amputate it until the darkened and decayed area disappears. Then force the wound open, for if the decay has reached a considerable extent and the decay has penetrated deep into the bone, there is no choice but to excise it.
Cut it (the part) to where the disease ends, then treat it until it heals. I inform you about a man who presented a case of gangrene in his thigh for demonstration and as an example for your treatment; this man was alive.
For about thirty years, he had a pain in his thigh caused by internal movement towards it from the body until the substances reached the thigh and caused severe swelling and inflammation, but there was no external cause. Then time passed with the mistake of doctors until the abscess opened.
From him, numerous substances emerged and his treatment was poor until the thigh turned gangrenous and many holes formed in it, all of which leaked pus and the body fluids; a group of doctors tried to treat him for about two years but none of them were skilled in surgery.
Until I intended to examine him, I saw his thigh and the substances flowing from those holes in large amounts and the man was lying on his bed with a pale body color; I inserted a probe into one of the holes and it passed through the bone then examined all the holes.
I found that some of them were connected to various parts of the thigh; when I probed one of the holes, I discovered part of the bone that was decayed and blackened, softened and crumbled until the hole reached the muscle; the decay spread wherever I uncovered it.
I am able to cure the rotten bone, and I think that there is no corruption in the bone other than what I have cut and dissected. I have definitely cured it, then made the wound take a longer time to heal with medicinal powders so that it did not harden. Then I counted, and found another layer of bone above the first examination.
I found the corruption connected to the bone, so I dissected what appeared to me of that corruption, then softened it with a tool without hardening. Then I examined it again and found that it had not healed. So I kept on cutting the bone piece by piece, and softening it without letting it heal until I reached a cavity in the bone.
Extracted it with a tool, then hardened it with medicine so that it healed quickly and recovered. However, this repetition is necessary in his work, as well as breaking it open due to its weakness and low probability, and fear of death since it would happen to him at every moment from the excessive bleeding which covered his wound and recovered completely.
Found a hard bone tissue in the location of the bone, and its condition was good in his body and it regained its strength and returned to normal functioning without any harm from the splinter, for if the bone was not solid in a part of the body, it would break.
It is required that a suture be drawn on this side and tightened on the soft tissue edge, then instruct someone to push it upwards. Another suture made from a stronger thread should then remain.
Then, suture the meat beneath the bone and pull its edge downwards to draw the meat upwards and expose the area you wish to stitch without harming the knife with the meat. Place a stick or flat stone under the bone firmly from below.
If you do that, the knife will not prevent the bone from being cut, and it is required that the stitch be above the defect slightly to avoid hollowing out the bone. It should not be too deep so as not to appear on the surface for palpation, requiring another stitch.
If the bone is rotten and not open, but there is a defect in its center or one of its sides, expose the meat from all sides, then place a stick from below, then stitch it from the first side where the defect is located.
If you finish stitching, stitch it from the other side and let the stitch be a little distance away from the defect as we said, if the defect is in a joint, then cut the diseased joint and sew the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the damage is in a separated joint, there is no remedy other than cauterization. If the damage is in the hand or leg tendon, treatment of it is very difficult. However, it should be incised and cauterized as it appears to you, then cleaned on any condition that you can manage.
With whatever means it is possible for you to reduce it when the artery or nerve does not oppose, and know that many cuts are made in these bones according to their position, and they are sawn, drilled, scraped, carved, stretched, shrunk, and hardened. Therefore, it is necessary to have a tool specifically for every type of work.
Know that the works themselves can lead to the type of tool required for them, if you have a long staff with knowledge of these crafts because mastery of these crafts is one of the skills necessary to see the afflictions. Chapter Seven and Eight.
On the amputation of limbs and the cutting of bones.
If the limb's extremity becomes gangrenous, either from external or internal cause, if treating the illness with medication does not cure it and the condition worsens within the limb without subsiding, then amputation is indicated.
It is necessary to amputate the affected limb up to the point where the gangrene has reached, thus saving the patient from death or extreme suffering which is greater than losing the limb. A sign that this should be done is when the limb turns black as if it were burnt by fire.
Or the gangrene may spread beyond the blackness, reaching further along the limb and entering the body, necessitating amputation. Similarly, if the cause of the gangrene is a wound from sea creatures such as sharks or crocodiles or rays and so on.
If the illness or wound is in the fingertip, amputate only the tip and do not delay as the gangrene may reach the hand. Similarly, if it has entered the hand, amputate from the wrist and again do not delay as the gangrene may advance to the forearm.
And likewise, if a fracture occurs in the arm, it should be cut at the joint in the middle, for it is permissible to allow foul play and you should pull it towards the neck, but do not cut the neck of the bone, for in this case there is death imminent. Instead, use another method of treatment according to your ability.
And likewise, if a fracture occurs in the finger, it should be cut at one of the knuckles. If a fracture occurs in the leg, then cut the leg at its tendon, and if the fracture is at the thigh, then cut the thigh at the hip. If there is an ascent to the knee, then cut the shin at the joint of the knee.
If the fracture has reached above the knee, there is no remedy except to leave it and let the patient die peacefully. The method of cutting or breaking the limb is to tie ropes below the site where you intend to cut, then apply tension to another rope above the site.
The one who holds the rope pulls it down, and the other who holds the upper rope pulls it upwards until you stretch the meat between the ropes until all of the meat is exposed. Then it is cut or broken, and it is preferable to break the bone completely.
If the saw is not placed correctly, it will cause excessive pain and burning, therefore if bleeding occurs during your procedure, quickly move the location or apply some hemostatic agents to stop the bleeding, then return to your treatment until you are finished.
Then tie the severed limb with a splint that is suitable for it and treat it until it heals, and I will inform you of an example of a case presented to me concerning a man in whose leg this similar case occurred: there was a blackness resembling fire in his leg.
This corruption first occurred in his finger, and the man took all of them, and when he saw the corruption spreading in the limb with great pain and burning, he amputated it at the joint, and it healed.
After a long time, he presented this corruption to me in his little finger of the hand with its tip swollen. He brought it to me, wrapped it with bandages containing the medicines that had been applied to his arm after cleaning the affected area. The corruption did not spread and made him try on the other finger.
He took the corruption in his hand and asked me to amputate it, fearing for himself from this corruption spreading, and also feared death due to the fall as he was on a steep slope. When I refused, he left for his country.
It has been reported to me that it was Baldur, who cut off his own hand with a rope, and he survived. I narrate this story as an example for what happens in this type of disease, and as a reference that one can rely on and act upon.
The Eighth Chapter and the Eighty.
On the Treatment of Abscesses and Methods of Injecting Medicines into Them.
If a boil occurs in one of the flesh-like body parts, and the duration of the boil increases until it collects pus, then either explodes or releases everything that was inside it and leaves the location empty as if it were an empty container.
And the skin that is like a blister, if it becomes inflamed but has not yet become severe, it does not affect bone, muscle, or nerves. From here it is appropriate to call it an abscess and not a boil unless its duration prolongs until the inflammation affects one of these body parts.
Then it is called a boil or a carbuncle, and its treatment is to cut off the entire skin layer that surrounds it, taking care not to tear it if it has become inflamed like a blister and you are certain that it will not adhere to the location due to the pus.
If a skin graft fails to adhere and there is no corruption, that amount (of tissue) which is in the skin contains a cyst of meat, it should be treated with cupping. Observe the cyst, if it is large and the unpleasant smell emanates from it, administer Egyptian medication by cupping.
Take honey, olive oil, and beeswax in any quantity and mix them together in a vessel. Cook on fire until the medicine solidifies into honey. Then take as much as you need, wet it with water and honey, draw it up in a cupping glass, apply it to the cyst, press it firmly, and leave the medicine inside for two hours.
Then, remove it in the evening (of that day). Repeat this process for several days until the cyst is cleaned and the stench disappears. If the above medicine does not work, draw up ashes of carob or ashes of fig tree leaves with water.
Clean it and continue cupping until you are certain that the cyst is cleaned, if there is a possibility of failure for both the Egyptian medicine and water ashes, draw up water and honey mixed with some powdered beeswax.
Examine it with honey and alcohol mixed, because it is the nature of honey to cleanse and purify, and alcohol penetrates the affected area well, especially if there is a deposit in it. If you do this several times and believe that the deposit has disappeared, test it again with what meat grows in it.
Take a piece of mature leeches, clean and rinse them with oil and wine or test it with some medicines and other known leeches that have proven properties in the article on leeches. If there is a narrowness in the affected area and the cannula does not fit, it means that the obstruction has been removed.
Stretch it slightly with iron or place inside it a pliable Egyptian leech or Syrian leech until it expands, and similarly if the mouth is also wide, gather both eyes with thread and leave only enough space for the cannula to enter without excess.
Similarly, if the mouth from which pus rises is elevated above, make a shallow place in it to drain the pus downwards because pus, when it adheres in the cavity of the affected area, prevents the meat from growing in it.
If you cannot break open the joint as intended below, then form a dislocation so that the limb may be easily restored to its original position without anything from the dislocated bone remaining trapped within it.
As for the medicated plasters placed on the joint from outside and the manner in which they should be applied to it, it is as follows: Take a medicated plaster suitable for your needs, roll it onto a matchstick until the matchstick is as long as the entire joint, so that it can fully cover it. Then, with a probe (mqs), make an incision in the joint, wider than the joint slightly. Then, using soft lint from the medicated plaster, roll it onto another matchstick to fit the size of the incision and place it on top, spaced away from the joint's opening to facilitate the removal of the dislocated bone and not remove the large incision immediately.
The number of days [to wait] and, as for the dressing on the joint, it is one that removes it at every moment to let out what has gathered in it, so you can know if anything from the dislocated bone is present – whether it's a lot or little, soft or hard.
If this also applies, does the patient feel pain in the place of the abscess or not? Is there no pain at all in the abscess? And is there swelling or not in the abscess? If your treatment proceeds like this and you observe the substance that comes out easy from the mouth of the abscess.
Know that the abscess has adhered or is about to adhere, so open it tomorrow or on the third day and whenever the incision was made, replace the instrument with another one and renew the dressing. If after a long period, a thin pus comes out from the abscess, do not be afraid of the swelling.
This pus is close at hand because some people stop the growth of flesh in their surgery and there are others who avoid this, if after many days a thick pus comes out which is not clear, know that the abscess did not adhere. If the abscess persists without being opened, it will rot.
The matter is lengthy, so let there be more in the drying of the medicines to make them retain moisture and have firmness similar to sticky plasters. If it increases, add more than the required amount of calamine. And that which is best treated with it is a sticky plaster.
The application of warm oil and vinegar is permissible on it, then the approved alcohol is poured onto it and kneaded with it, and it is used. If fresh fig honey is not available, you can cook it until it becomes white and use it or take mustard, salt, pepper, and grind them all together and leave it on the honey that is on fire. Then grind it on a flat surface or grind with honey that has been cooked until it becomes white, place the figs on top of it and press down.
In such cases, long Zarwand (a type of herb), Asafoetida, powdered cinnamon, and Ginger can be used individually or together. They are crushed, kneaded into fig honey (as previously described) and applied. If the wound persists for a long time and does not respond to this treatment, know that there is corruption in it.
It has an effect on bones or other hard tissues and joints, treat it with Nasaur treatment as described.
The ninth and the twenty-eighth chapters.
On the treatment of fistulas, abscesses that persist, and cutting off excess fingernails and removing calluses.
The Dahas is a large tumor that grows under the skin of the hand or leg, and sometimes in other fingers; if it persists and treatment is neglected, it causes severe pain and swelling.
The dahas can also lead to decay and rot, causing the skin to eat away until it reaches the bone and emits a foul odor. It may even cause complete decay of all the fingers, or the decay may spread to the bone such that the tip of the finger becomes exposed and its color turns black. If treated with what has been described in the division [part] and if there is no improvement, the treatment has failed.
It is necessary to cut all the pus that grows from the skin using iron, then suture the wound afterwards as this method is highly effective. If the bone and the skin are both healthy.
The angle of the tumor has drawn the flesh into it and blocked it, it is necessary to place a thin piece of iron under this angle and raise it up, then cut the affected meat along with it.
Place the burning, consuming medicine on what remains of the flesh until it disappears completely, then treat it with a purgative to make it discharge. If decay has occurred in the bone, it is necessary to cut and remove that bone as it will not heal while any remaining bone is rotten.
If you find an ulcer and corruption in the thumb, cut it in one of the joints as it is mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the bone (of the thumb) is struck or broken and severe pain occurs, it is necessary to remove the cause first.
Then, make a sharp cut on the bone obliquely from above to below and prevent it from reaching the flesh beneath the bone, for by doing so you cause pain and damage due to the fracture and there will be a buildup of extra tissue at the site. Treat the wound accordingly.
As for the excessive thumbs that grow on some people, it may not be entirely made of flesh or it may contain bone in some cases, or there may be a bone and a nail growing separately from one of the fingers.
Or it may grow in some of the joints (between fingers) that do not move, and it may grow at the joint separating the fingers. If it is not made of flesh, simply cut it near its base with a straight incision.
As for the one whose root is in the palm, its cure is difficult and it should be excised with care. And as for the one that grows in the nail of one of the Slamites [slaves], it is necessary to first cut off its flesh and then spread the bone with a suitable saw.
Then cure it until it heals, and as for the adhesion that affects some fingers with each other, it often occurs and is one of those things that may cause harm to man, especially due to injury or burns and similar things. It is necessary to break this adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural position, then place something between them, either a thread or a splint soaked in oil of roses so that it does not adhere quickly and separates them. Or make a thin lead sheet between them until the situation is as it should be.
And similarly, if adhesion occurs between some fingers and the wrist, break that adhesion according to what is appropriate and it will heal in the shape of the limb.
The chapter is forty.
On amputation.
The amputation is the cutting of swollen, hard, blackened ulcers that occur in most parts of the body and are mostly found in the feet, toes, and hands. It is necessary to use leeches from the black one several times powerfully.
Then extract the bladder of the animal, as for its treatment with iron, it will be on two strikes: one to make it break and black blood and the other to make it seep and extract it along with the urine, if it breaks then it should be heated first with hot water.
Allow the thick blood to congeal, then draw the probe from above and hold it below with a thread, then break the bladder in one or two or three places, widely.
Draw out the black blood from below the probe to above and from above to below until you extract the desired amount of blood that is sufficient or as much as the strength of the probe allows, then tie it and order it to abstain from food associated with the black color, and allow it to rest and recover.
When the bladder becomes full and is damaged by the probe, if there is a lot of hair in it, then cut off the probe, immerse it in hot water until it turns red or undergoes strong exercise.
If the water bath is not available until the limb becomes hot, then break the skin around the bladder, either at the end of the probe or at its base, then open the skin with scissors and draw out the urine from all sides until it can be felt.
When it appears red and swollen, if it is removed from the skin, it appears like a bladder; then enter a leech under it until it rises and comes out of the skin; tie it with a silk thread, then make another incision near that one, as far as three fingers.
Then cut the skin from above the vein until it appears, then lift it with a leech as you did earlier and tie it again with another silk thread, just as you did first; then make another incision near that one, or several incisions if necessary.
Then pull it out and cut it at the end of the incision at the armpit, then draw it out from the second incision, then draw it back to the first incision and repeat this until you draw it out from the third incision at the topmost armpits; when all of them have come out, cut it.
If you are not required to draw it out and tie it, insert a strong thread-like probe and secure it, then introduce a leech under it with your hand in all directions until it comes out and is preserved so that it does not break; if it breaks, it will make it difficult for you to pull it out.
The excerpt translates to: "When he (the patient) experiences poisoning, if all of it leaks out, place him on the operation table with a warm woolen cloth soaked in oil or fat and treat him until he recovers. If the case is serious with symptoms spreading, administer leeches at the affected areas.
"Do not press too hard as we have previously mentioned, but you should apply pressure to each of the affected areas and their sites, then bind them with bandages until they are all drained. Ensure that during the process of draining, neither the vein is cut nor injured because it will make it difficult for you to drain it, thus saving your effort.
