The lever is used to strike the bone in order not to injure the skull, for if the bone is hard and solid it should be drilled before using the pieces with the smooth chisels, which are called non-rough chisels.
This is because it does not exceed the depth of the skull bone beyond that point, for the chisel has a sharp tip similar to the bevel or small circle which prevents it from gouging and over-penetrating the bone.
It is necessary for you to choose many of these clamps numerous, each one suitable for the size of that bone until it brings for every joint a clamp of its side length and shortening according to the size of that joint, and this is a picture of three types. The three types are large clamps, medium clamps, and small clamps.
The method of clamping the broken bone is to place the clamp on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been cut through, then move the clamp to another location and make a distance between each clamp equal to the thickness of the rope or approximately.
Then, cut between the two clamps and do so until you have joined the bone either with your hand or with another tool from some of the instruments that you have prepared for this purpose.
For example, like the pliers and delicate forceps, it is necessary to avoid any danger of the clamp or cutter touching the marrow, so if the bone is covered with a layer of marrow and it adheres to it and you remove it, it is necessary to clean it.
"Smooth out the ulcer that remains in the bone with another tool resembling it, except that it should be sharper and softer than the others. If there remains something of small bones or shards, take them away with whatever tools you have.
"Then treat the wound with the scalpel and forceps as previously mentioned, and regarding the other aspect of the procedure, it is easy to extract a foreign body by means remote from danger, as Galen praised and highly commended this method. "First, you should start by excavating the bone fragment from the site of the fracture with the strongest and sharpest tool until you expose that piece of bone, then you should look beneath it for this broken fragment whose image is as follows:
"The broken fragment will be soft and not cut anything, while the sharp edge of it is on its movable sides in length as you can see. The soft fragment serves as a guide for the chisel, and the sharp edge of the broken piece in the bone should be struck from one side.
"With a small hammer, you should cut all the bone until it breaks with safety, as the fracture will not be dangerous. Even if the person performing the operation is inexperienced or clumsy.
If there remains anything that adheres to the bone in some positions of the bone, then shatter it with the pointed part of the instrument and remove it along with it. It will be removed without harm or fear.
If the bone is broken and does not reach the adhesion, and there remains a part of the bone with roughness and gristle on its surface, then it is necessary to excise that roughness and grind those gristles flat. You may take different measures from it for each case according to what guides you in the work and cure it accordingly.
The broken bone and its shape, it is necessary to first straighten the splinters then use something coarser afterwards, and do not stop until you have made it suitable for further use by smoothing and sharpening all of it.
As for the small, hairy fractures and fine breaks, it is necessary to treat each one according to what leads to its healing, which is not hidden from one who has knowledge in this craft. The lowest level of care and stopping at what has been written and clearly explained regarding large fractures.
If the wound remains open during bone setting, it is necessary to take a probe of suitable size and insert it into the marrow and flesh and place it on the wound site, then take another probe, either round or triangular. And insert it into a liquid mixture of marrow, fat and blood, place it on the first probe, then use a broad bandage that does not tighten except to hold the wound closed.
Leave it with this treatment for a few days until the hot inflammation subsides, then dissolve it and use medicine that contains ingredients that solidify such as root of sassafras, cinnamon powder, ginger powder, cardamom and similar substances, making a poultice from these medicines.
Keep the wound as clean as possible during treatment, for this reason use medicine that whitens and does not blacken. Make an effort during your treatment of the wound to ensure it remains clean, without grease, pus or accumulation of maggots.
Because maggots, if they gather on the skull wound, will rot and weaken it greatly, and this can lead to a very serious complication when the bone is exposed during surgery, especially if proper treatment is neglected.
Swelling on the surface of observation, if it shows symptoms as we have mentioned, then know that there is no remedy for it; however, if swelling only occurred due to a medicine placed upon it and the strength of that medicine was such that it could cause this swelling, then this swelling can be removed by weakening or completely removing the medication.
It is necessary to take three parts from honey and three parts of beeswax, grind them well and melt them together. Then apply this mixture to the plaster and treat it with the appropriate treatment until it heals.
Third chapter.
Treating a broken nose.
Know that the nose does not break except by a complete crack of its upper part or one of its parts because the part below it is cartilage and does not break; instead, it bends, twists, and swells.
If one of the cracks is broken, it is necessary to insert small fingers into the nose and reset the break from inside with your little finger and thumb until the nose returns to its natural shape; this should be done carefully so that you do not cause any additional pain or damage to the patient.
If the break is in the upper part of the nose and has not reached the tip, it should be sutured with a thin wire-like material. If the break is on both sides, do as mentioned above, and apply pressure daily on the first day of the break.
If a fever is present on the seventh or tenth day, then enter through the nose a probe made of cedar wood if the break is in one part, or two probes if the break is in both parts.
Make the thread rough in size sufficient to fill the nose cavity, and some of the early physicians have said that one should smear the thread with oil each day; however, I do not see that as correct, rather it is preferable to smear the thread with white egg yolk mixed with wheat flour.
Leave the thread until the bone heals and the cartilage hardens; then insert into the nose tubes made from the yew tree after they have been treated with a soft, light substance to hold them in place for breaking the nose. This will make the splint stronger and prevent the fracture from healing improperly.
This is not necessary if you decide to make the thread or sutures; simply expose the nose and use a hot poultice or bandage soaked in a concoction made of camphor, cotton, wrapped in linen and/or something similar used by pharmacists.
If a boil does not burst, it should be lanced from outside with precise cautery of salt and lime, which has been well ground with egg white. Then place a poultice made of soft flour over it and do not bind the nostrils with anything, for if the bones of the nose are broken, they will break in small pieces.
If it opens up, it should be probed and drained with an appropriate tool then the wound should be stitched and treated with a remedy that agrees with it. If there is an injury inside the nose, it should be treated with a lancet and copper tubes should be used. Until it breaks open.
The fourth section.
If the lower jaw is fractured.
If the lower jaw is fractured but not accompanied by injury in the face, if it is fractured from the outside and does not extend into the interior, then its recognition is easy.
If the fracture is in the right side, a splint made from the left hand should be inserted into the mouth of the patient and likewise if the fracture is in the left side, a splint made from the right hand should be inserted and lift the fractured piece from inside to outside.
Apply the other instrument from outside the bone to control it, if it's a broken jaw, it should be set with two fingers in alignment. If it has been fractured in the teeth, it will be displaced.
Use an instrument such as gold wire, silver or brass to tie it, then place the splint made of quarry stone on the broken bone, and place a large, firm plaster or a piece of thick leather equal in length to the bone. Then tie it from above as you see fit to secure it, keeping it in alignment so that it does not dislocate. The patient should rest and remain immobile, and feed him only soft, bland food. If you suspect a change in its shape in any direction, make sure to check it the next day.
Then tie it from above as you see fit to secure it, keeping it in alignment so that it does not dislocate. The patient should rest and remain immobile, and feed him only soft, bland food. If you suspect a change in its shape in any direction, make sure to check it the next day. Treat the fractured area with lime plaster mixed with egg white or fine flour after removing the splint, then place a soft padding on top of it. If the padding remains stuck to it and the bone does not change, leave it be.
Do not open it (the wound) until he breathes deeply and the fracture widens considerably, for this fracture often widens within three weeks. If there is a hot abscess appearing during that period, apply what was previously mentioned to treat it repeatedly until the abscess disappears.
If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and it appears that the bone has been displaced with shards or splinters, be gentle in removing these shards using an instrument of your choice to extract them. If there is a narrowing in the wound, widen it gently with a probe.
Then, if you have removed the shards and there remains nothing more from them, apply a compress on the wound if it is wide enough, otherwise place one of the dressings that suit the case. Warm the wound until it breathes.
Fifth Section.
In case of fracture, apply traction.
A fracture usually occurs at the end of a long bone, and it can break in one of three directions: either it breaks and splinters, or it happens without any splintering, which is easier to apply traction to.
Or if the fracture occurs with splinters, it is more difficult to apply traction, and if the fracture occurs without a wound, one must gather assistants to hold the bone segment being pulled on tightly while the other pulls in the opposite direction.
Then, set the fracture right with your fingers until its shape is as desired and there is no protrusion or sinking, if it requires a longer time, it is necessary to place under the broken bone a ball of linen or cotton.
Adjust the bone as necessary and pull the traction, raise the tissue and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is set in its desired form. If you are unable to draw the end of the tissue out because it has gone too deep, it is necessary to extract the broken bone from one side.
Place a splint made of moderate size under the fracture and make the caretaker pull it down until the protruding bone rises to its correct position above. Then, adjust and set the fracture with your fingers.
If you suspect that a piece of the tissue has been fractured and is moving, it is necessary to press on it and extract the broken piece along with it. If the broken piece is embedded in the bone, it must be divided into pieces for extraction. After the traction device is placed under the bone, the instrument for holding the probe should be made of wood or metal and its shape is as follows:.
The cup resembles [it], but it has no excavation. Its width is according to the size of the bone required and its length according to what you can manage, and it should have two sides like the wider one you see or the narrower one.
If the incision that you made for the removal of bone splinters is wide and the pain is severe, gather the wound with suturing, and if the incision is easy or you are afraid of the severe pain, anesthetize the wound with the incision and as much as the incision allows.
If there is severe pain, anesthetize the incision with oil, honey or alcohol and carry it, and if the bone is not injured or not fractured, hold on to the adhesive taken from charcoal powder with white egg. Place the lint on it, then place the ball under its bandage if necessary, then take a long stick that is narrow or about that and hold two ligatures and the adhesive on the wound, then take a sharp knife. Its width should be three fingers and in length likewise, then arrange it in the incision, then arrange that incision with the knife in the position of the fracture, then tighten the adhesive on the fracture as it rotates. The fracture should be set on the neck and beneath the articular surface of both the healthy and the diseased bone. It should be manipulated in all directions, and you should see if the pressure stabilizes the fracture firmly; this is something that should not escape your notice.
The ligature must not be removed from the fractured bone. It is recommended that the ligature be examined every day, and each time it is found that the ligature has slipped, adjust it accordingly and tighten the ligature again. Then let the patient sleep on his back with a small pillow under his neck at night.
The bone should be raised with it to elevate the fracture along with the upward movement of the limb, or the arm should be tied to the neck, and the ligature should not be loosened unless there is an accidental or traumatic event occurring within 14 days.
Then change the dressing if you notice any signs for that purpose. Apply pressure and leave it until the fracture becomes stable, strengthening more within 42 days. It may be so in some people in a shorter time period.
Sixth Chapter.
Sixth Chapter: Fracture of the Collarbone. If the collarbone cracks at its broadest part, it does not fracture completely but rather breaks some of its letters. But if it ever cracks at a point or cracks in the middle, this is known by touch. Its form depends on what kind of crack it has.
Adjust it and put it back to its natural shape from every direction you can, then place on the site a mixture of potsherd with egg white and soft glue. Then place over it a flat sheet of bone.
Scrape the collarbone gently to its full length or slightly more than it, if there is a hollow beneath the plate in one of the collarbone sites, fill that hollow with soft glue until the plate lies flat, then fasten from above with a long pin.
Tighten and fasten the plate firmly so that it does not come off its place, and check the screws every day. Whenever a screw comes loose, tighten it again and adjust the plate if it comes off from its place, but make sure the injury is on the correct side of the collarbone, as the collarbone heals in 20 days.
If [it is] a day or twenty-two days, and when this number has passed, then loosen the bandages, and you are safe; for it is of the bones which there is no fear of breaking nor splintering. If any piece of bone protrudes beneath the skin, break it and remove it. Make what you have described in the treatment of broken bone for relieving a hot worm if something like that occurs.
Section Seven.
On the Treatment of Broken Neck.
The neck may break in the middle slightly, but it is less likely to do so and its sides are more prone to breaking and dislocating. Among its symptoms when it breaks in the middle are that the head bends downwards, exposing it to pain, difficulty in breathing, and suffocation.
If it bleeds and the fracture is visible in the bone, bind it with a bandage that presses against the fractured area and covers it entirely, then gradually apply pressure from both sides while adjusting it as needed on all sides to straighten it.
Compress it until the bone's shape returns to its desired form, then place a plaster or similar material on top of it, securely covering it with bandages after breaking it in a crack. Then, you should be gentle when tying [it] to the broken bone so it does not slip, and pass the suture in a circular motion toward the chest several times and tighten it firmly. Then, check the suture at every moment, and whenever it becomes loose, tighten it again.
If it should be necessary to treat a wound, ulcer or abscess, then treat it and loosen the bandage, cure what is exposed of that by the treatment method appropriate for it, then return the bandage if you see fit. Turn it over and press firmly until it comes out.
The eighth section.
In the treatment of fractures, know that fractures occur in places that are close to the chest and along its sides because they are weak points. The swelling [due to the fracture] is evident to touch during examination by fingers.
Press it back into place with fingers on the most suitable side until its shape is as desired, then bind and compress the broken bone tightly if necessary. If a fracture is near internal organs, it may lead to serious swelling or pain.
We make an incision similar to the one that exposes a person for amputation, so that the bone will break the covering and also expose it to pain, difficulty in breathing, excessive bleeding, and this makes the treatment difficult; and the beginning of it is often full of complications.
Some of them say it is necessary to make the diet of the patient produce flatulence and wind, so that the abdomen swells and expands and fracture is forced outwards; but we dislike this because it may lead to the occurrence of inflammation if no inflammation occurs; if inflammation has occurred, it increases and strengthens it.
Some of them say a plug should be placed at the site, then it should be pressed with strength, which is similar to measuring except that it fears that the plug will attract pus to the site in case of weakness; some of them say it is necessary for the site to be covered with a cloth soaked in hot oil.
The wound will heal between the incisions until it is filled, and if the ligature is loosened on a rotating basis, then the patient should be treated with food and medicine for wounds; for the patient's condition is severe and cannot endure it. The bone is sensitive to the touch, it becomes painful and we fear a fracture, it is necessary that we make an incision at the location and expose the broken part, then we should put the tool beneath it which preserves the tip and describe it, and cut the bone along with it and extract it.
We collect the wounded eyes if it is large by sewing, and treat it with cautery until it bleeds, for if it opens during this process and a hot wound occurs, then we should apply mustard on the spot and treat the inflammation from within as well.
It is taken on the side that avoids sleep until it heals.
The ninth chapter.
Cauterizing of the forehead and neck.
If the bones of the neck are fractured or have a slight exposure, and also if there is a risk of infection, and the same applies to the skull fractures, if it is exposed to someone and you want to know whether it heals or not. Look, if you see that his hands have become cold and pale, and they are swollen and cannot be moved or stretched or squeezed, and if you try to squeeze or pierce them with a needle, but he does not feel it and finds no pain in them, then know that it is mostly not healing, and it will perish.
If it moves them both and he feels bone scraping and grinding, know that the fracture of the bone has settled and that the patient is ready for treatment. However, if you find a crepitus in the back, similar to this, and want to learn whether it also recovers or not, it does recover as well.
Look at his legs, if you see that they are fractured and the same thing happened in the hands, then when he lies on his back, gas and pus come out without intention, and when he presses on his abdomen, urine comes out without intention. If he presses on his back and desires to urinate but cannot, know that he is dying, so do not treat him. For if nothing of this kind has happened to him, the matter is better. The treatment for what happened is to relieve pain by cooling the hot wound.
Place warm oil of almond or with white lard on the inflamed bladder and let it stay for three times during the day until the hot pain subsides. Then, put one of the binding bandages made of clean cloths on the spot and tighten it with rope, order him to rest and decide.
A man shall not sleep except on the side where he finds no pain until it relieves, for if something has occurred in a bone during anger, or if something has been relieved from it, it is necessary to incise the skin over it and remove that bone. Then gather the wounded eyes if they are large with sewing, then treat it with warm pigeon droppings until it relieves. If the last bone of the big toe is broken and it's the heel bone, it is necessary to apply pressure from the left hand in the hollow part.
Straighten the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible, and then apply a splint and bandage if necessary, then set it.
If you feel a fracture in it, incise and remove it, and treat the wound with the appropriate treatment until it relieves.
The Tenth Chapter.
On the Treatment of a Broken Nail.
When nails are broken, it is only a break if it splits along the sides and cracks in length, bends towards inside and exposes the place and pain occurs at the site, and the finger swells and turns black from that side.
Thou must roll thy hand upon it until thou reach the break, and when its shape is known to thee; if the break be at the sides only, then set that break in order according to what appears suitable for mending it to resemble its natural shape.
If the break be in length or if it has been displaced within, place a splint on its belly until it becomes stable for thee to set that break. Once set, apply bandages to it, then place a splint made of wood or leather and tie it strongly.
Fear not for the break to move nor for the splint to slip, and mend the fracture from the splinters that fill it until the tie takes its proper shape. Order the patient to lie on his back or on the correct side, for he will feel hot and painful if exposed.
Loosen the bandages and set the fracture until the hot pain subsides, then place on it what soothes it according to what is available, then return to setting and tying as required. If there are splinters in the bone or a crack from its sides, do not remove them.
Do not touch but set it from outside as we have said and leave the tie until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
In Surgery: Fracture of the Bone.
The bone is from the shoulder joint to the end of the limb; if it is fractured, you should set it on two sides; one side shall hold a straight rod with a medium thickness in this manner:.
And tie rods on either side, then from a high place he sits and the fracture patient leans over the rod; then he puts his broken arm on the rod so that its forearm becomes stuck in the middle of the rod's curve, then something heavy is placed above it.
Or the servant lowers [the patient] and the doctor adjusts the fracture with his hands until the fracture aligns as intended, and the other side is for the fracture patient to lean on his elbow and tie his hand from his neck with a rope.
Then order the servants to press gently on either side of the fracture, one from above and the other from below; each should pull towards his side, and if you wish it to be more secure, tighten a rope under the fracture and place one above it, and let each servant pull towards their respective sides.
If the fracture is near the elbow, it is necessary that the center of the rope be under the forearm and the other end under the fracture in the direction of the joint; similarly, if the fracture is near the joint, it should be on that place and on the joint itself.
Then, the fracture shall be set with a gentle pressure until it is level as required and allowed to heal naturally. If it does not develop a hot pus, leave it as it is. But if it develops a hot pus, wait for the seventh day, then place a soft cloth soaked in honey and oil of wormwood on it.
Then, if the pus disappears, set the fracture. The dressing should be removed from the fracture, then a piece of fresh plaster should be placed on it. Gather the limb to the body and place the hand open on the plaster.
Wrap the fracture and the cast on the limb, so that the limb stands in the position of the bones. If it does not prevent you from doing this and if it does not change anything in the broken bone, use plaster. If you fear that something might change in it, use splints.
Put splints on the fracture, which should be straight and stronger than other splints, and make a space between each splint so that there is an interval of about three fingers' width for each side of the splint.
Then press firmly on the fracture as described in the first part of this chapter.
And it should be firm pressure at the site of the break.
And after the fracture, if you see the position of the bones and the pressure as we have described earlier when you press the limb from your hour, do that.
If you fear the hot inflammation, leave the pressure and bones till the seventh day as we have mentioned earlier. Then examine the bandages every three days so that no pain or wind or food reaches the limb due to the continued pressure. This will heal everything, as we have described.
If you are confident that nothing will happen in this case, do not loosen the bandages except after many days. The swelling should be on top of the affected area and the hand should be placed on its side under the limb, covered with a cushion filled with evenly distributed cotton.
Examine at every opportunity, day or night, so that the shape of the broken limb does not change or the bandages weaken. This will heal everything with your effort, and make the food for the affected area gentle at first until the bones have healed.
It is necessary that food softens, for usually the intestines and veins harden in forty days, then it is necessary that it dissolves and the hot bath is used and it is treated with appropriate poultices, if the break is severe.
Do not dissolve bandages from it for more than fifty days or two months.
Chapter thirteen.
In the book on broken bone.
The bone is composed of two bones and it is called the ulna, one of them is smaller and lies above the elbow, and the other is larger and is below the smaller one; rarely does the larger bone break on its own or the smaller one breaks on its own, and rarely do they both break together.
When the smaller upper bone breaks, its healing is easy and it heals faster; when the lower bone breaks, its break is difficult and healing is slower, and if both bones break, then the bone which caused the smaller upper bone to break is more likely to be the one that broke.
It is necessary for the physician when treating a broken bone that he should make it easier to move, until it is set; if the larger bone is the one that broke, then it is necessary that he makes it stronger; and if both bones are broken, then it is necessary that he makes it much stronger.
The hand shape should be placed under the patient and stretched out straight on a board. The hand should be bound above all the fingers, with the little finger being lower than the others and the thumb standing erect on itself.
The board should be raised to a level such that the patient does not suffer difficulty.
Then the attendant holding the limb shall pull it from below either with his hand or with a rope, and the other assistant shall pull from above in the same manner. Then the physician should adjust the bone until it is aligned properly. If there is a fracture of the bone, grating sounds will be heard.
Align each fragment in its place with effort, for if a free-floating fragment appears and it pierces the skin without causing much pain, break it in the manner described earlier, for if the fracture is accompanied by injury, you will have failed to set it correctly.
Take treatment for this from there, if the joint is swollen and hot at first, make an incision with a lancet made of boar's bristles or white camel hair, keep it moderate between piercing and scraping, press firmly until the pain subsides.
Extract the pus, place a clean dressing made of ashes from the mill mixed with egg white. Then pick up the forceps and make it strong and slightly inclined. Know that the number of forceps in the arm is usually four.
The fracture can be in one bone or in two bones together, then make your grip stronger on the location of the fracture and make it stronger and firmer, and whenever you increase the strength, move it slightly higher or lower, a little bit closer to what was mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
Make the probe soft and not too hard, and let the thread that holds it be moderate between being rough and smooth as we have described. Also check the limb and the ligament after some days.
If something happens that needs repair, such as a swelling in the limb, it is necessary to immobilize the limb with warm water until the swelling settles and then leave it free for the night until it relaxes, and then reapply the grip.
If the grip has relaxed and the bone has settled or something similar, repair it all with your effort, also look to see if food is prevented from reaching the limb due to the tightness of the grip, in that case, you should leave it for a short while and wait for food to reach it, then reapply the grip.
If the patient has not been affected by anything we have mentioned, it is not advisable to perform surgery except after twenty days or around that period, then tie the arm of the patient to his neck so that the elbow is level, and he should maintain effort from forced movements and make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm will heal in thirty days or two and a half months, or it may heal in eighty-two days, all according to the conditions of the patient and his strength.
The third chapter.
On the subject of breaking the hand and fingers.
The fracture of the hand and finger joints is more exposed to rupture than to breaking, but when does the hand break or is it ruined?
The patient should sit erect and in front of him a chair on a level surface.
Then, he should place his hand on the chair and the servant of bones will be summoned to arrange them, and the doctor will do so until they align properly. If there is no complication or hot swelling, then a bandage and splint should be applied. After that, a cushion from above should be placed according to the situation.
"If the break is below the wrist, make a ball-shaped suture from the thread and instruct the assistant to grasp it with the broken hand, then press firmly with the suture thread.
"To make a suture from tendon, smooth the skin over all fingers and press it with force as required, if the break is outside then make one suture above and another below in the hand so that the hand remains open.
"Then, the pressure is applied as the hand turns and the fingers are interlaced with a rope. If the break is visible between any of the fingers, if there is swelling then it should be treated appropriately, then press with the hand and if desired, make a small suture to hold the break.
"If the break is in all fingers such as the middle, thumb, index or little finger, it should be treated and then sutured to the correct finger or all of them are sutured together on a splint, this is best, or a small suture can be placed on it.
"As was said before, in case of swelling and abscess, apply what is needed during the time of pressure and after it has passed from the hot fever, take appropriate measures whenever such occurs.
Fourth Chapter Ten.
In Surgery: Fracture of the Bone.
The Bone of the Leg is frequently Fractured and it is easily felt as it deviates towards the front and back, and its fracture will be by tension above the crack and tension below the crack, and the fractured bone should be set in place.
Then each ligature should be pulled towards its end in proportion to this (procedure), if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But, if it is near the base of the limb, it is necessary to tighten the ligatures either with sinew or something similar.
At the base of the limb, the pulling should be towards above and the other ligature below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the joint, the ligatures should be near the joint and pulled downwards. Then the physician should align the bone with both hands until it assumes its natural shape.
The bone should be broken carefully, then it is necessary that the ligatures and compression are applied unless there is pain or swelling in the limb. If there is swelling, wait until the heat subsides before returning to treatment.
If the compression is tightened, it is necessary to loosen it on the fracture with a hot and wide tool twice or thrice, leaving some slack, then tie the thread so that the knot is at the end of the limb, and insert a long cord between the bone and the flesh near the joint from below, and make the ends of the cord on both sides.
Then, he should turn the leg and thigh well, more than usual examination, then make a probe at the place of break on the thigh and make it into a single probe, and put it in the bone of the leg, then fill the gap between the thigh and the leg with soft wax to equalize the tension.
Then, he should start with tightening the strengths from the center at the place of break, three or four twists, and every time he advances with a loop from the place of break, his tension should become less, softer, and looser.
Then, he should go to the ends of the string that you have inserted between the thigh and the leg, tie it with what is above, then pass by the end of the string towards below until it reaches the ankle, tighten both ends of the string along with the other probes from the other side.
In order for the loop not to fall off from its place, then leave the tension on it until it is no longer affecting the limb with eating or pain or breathing or similar things, for if something like this happens, rectify it and correct everything that happened due to it according to what we mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a splinter in the bone, it must be removed if possible, and if not, break it and remove it, and treat the injury with similar methods until it heals, but this fracture of the thigh can be reinforced without adding the leg with probes.
As we have mentioned in the limb and the bone, except that its dislocation is not exposed to any cause for it to protrude, even if it has been dislocated; there is no doubt that the owner of it must hold it constantly.
Know that the fracture (of a bone) lasts about fifty days, or may increase slightly or decrease slightly, all of this is subject to differences in the composition and various conditions.
The fifth chapter.
On the fracture of a horse's pelvis.
A fracture in the horse's pelvis is somewhat exposed to fractures and it is often exposed to rupture greatly, so if it is exposed to a fracture, it will be either a crack or splitting in some of its parts and this occurs with injury or without injury.
Hold on to all of that by touch, and dislocate it so that what has been separated from its parts comes together with the fingers until they are united and bound together according to what is possible for their union and bonding and certainty, then support the bandage and place a sling around it if it is necessary.
The pressure from the fracture that is suitable for this (procedure) persists, then it accommodates all conditions we have described in other fractures such as inflammation and similar situations by facing each problem with what suits it to heal until it heals completely.
Sixteenth Chapter.
On Jaw Fracture.
On the Bone of the Jaw, there are two bones; one is thick and is called the bone of the jaw, and the other is thin and is called zanda. Both are subject to various types of fracture, except for that which affects the bone of the arm. Therefore, fracturing the jaw is similar to arm fracture in terms of treatment; if both bones are fractured, the jaw will be displaced in all directions. If only the thin bone is fractured, the jaw will be displaced towards the front, and if the thick bone is fractured alone, which can occur from below, it is obvious.
Use the splint, alignment, and binding of the jawbones accordingly; except that if the fracture of the jaw is a comminuted one with many fragments, it is advisable to make the splint smaller and thinner, and to exert your effort on the jaw.
In the jaw, there is an additional step beyond the arm; when you have aligned and completed all your work, take two cups of snakebite wine which are used in the treatment of wounds or made from date pits or similar materials.
Choose from it that which has a little toughness and is not brittle, and its length should be equal to the length of the thumb from the wrist to below. Then, break each one into two pieces on its length, and place one part on the thumb and the other part on the opposite side.
From the wrist to below the foot, then bind the splints in three places on both sides and in the middle. In this way, the thumb is prevented from bending inward, outward, or upward excessively. A measuring stick made of wood can be used for the length of the thumb.
Place it to keep it from movement, and it is best to do that when there is a specific injury. Then, examine the thumb every day with great care to prevent inflammation or infection or anything else. If something like this happens, apply what is necessary until it heals, and the thumb joint should be immobilized for about thirty days or close to that.
The seventh chapter.
On fractures of bones and fingers.
As for the elbow, it is not subject to the fracture of the biceps bone and as for the bones of the leg, they can be subjected to a fracture. The fingers also have less susceptibility to a fracture, but they are more prone to dislocation in most cases.
If the leg bone is exposed and you see some of the bones protruding against each other, place your foot on the ground at an angle like a donkey's and then you place your foot on what rises above those bones, press and straighten it.
Until it returns to its place, then take the bandages and splints from above and place them under the sole of the foot on a small flat surface with two concave sides, then press firmly on it with force after breaking it with the levers and whatever else is needed.
If it has been three or four days and the leg bone is set, release the bandages. You will find the bones are level as they were broken or displaced. The work is then done as you see. As for if some of the fingers are broken, force them straight according to what you have described in the art of finger manipulation.
Then make a splint for the broken fingers of the same length as the finger and slightly protruding from it, then place this board under the foot which you have described, and press it firmly with great force, for if a bone in the leg is broken in two or more places.
Apply a blunt probe of suitable size, placed in the incision, on every toe; let each head of the probe protrude externally from the foot so that it may serve as a handle for fine adjustment.
It is obligatory for you to follow all that we have mentioned regarding the fracture, including all its possible symptoms that we have described, as well as what is required for each symptom.

The ninth chapter.
In setting bone fractures with injury.
It is necessary for the one who presents with a fracture accompanied by injury, and especially if the bone is large such as the phalanx or ribs or similar, to make every effort to separate it from its time when you can meet the conditions of separation as we have mentioned.
If the wound bleeds, it is necessary to apply a powdered cautery to it. If there's no assistant, do it yourself immediately.
If a hot inflammation does not occur, do nothing. But if a hot inflammation occurs, leave the cauterization for nine days until the hot inflammation subsides and do not approach it on the third and fourth day as it may show adverse effects.
If the fractured bone protrudes through the skin, it is necessary to reduce and set it with your hands along with a gentle pull. But if reduction and setting with your hands does not work, reduce and set it with this instrument, which is an instrument made of iron about seven or eight fingers in length. Expose it to the wound.
The physician should take from it (the fracture) three or four pieces, depending on what treatment is required for each type of fracture, and he should roll them up so that they are contained in a narrow space, with a little glue to prevent it from breaking when squeezed during the operation.
The edges of the instrument should be sharp with a concave shape on one side and convex on top, and its bottom half should be smooth and well-tempered, as shown below:.
The Yunanian peri (small knife) is named thus because it aims for a small incision, making its tip sharp and directing it towards the adjacent bone, then pushing with it until the bone returns and levels with some of the surrounding areas; thus, the fractured edges are aligned one with another.
If the fractured edge is sharp and the instrument does not grasp it properly, it is necessary to cut that bone until the instrument can take hold of it. If it cannot return the bone as described by our method, then cut it with whatever cutters we have mentioned.
If you can't use one of the saws, then cut it as you are able to. After that, remove any roughness or shards remaining in the bone. If the bone returns and there is a hole left behind after its return and the pain is severe and distressing, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to return it to its natural position, you will be of great benefit to the patient. If you have exerted your strength on the bone, then immerse a splinter in a dark, specific liquor – if you are in summer – and do not put cotton or anything with oil on the wound.
Do not let anything putrefy or decay in it, then use the forceps when you are released from the bone's grip and leave the wound open so that it may be cauterized with a probe in its channels as much as necessary for the wound.
Be careful not to let the wound break too much for this often causes problems in their sickness, either death or gangrene or ulcers. Let your cauterization be soft and gentle, opposing that of other fractures.
If the wound is small or large and you fear some minor side effects that we have described, and if it becomes inflamed in the place, do not apply forceps and do not make channels for lead to be placed into their location and cauterize it.
If after a day or two, you notice that the wound begins to grow pus, then remove the probe which was placed in it with liquid and use the lancets and leeches as we usually cauterize wounds like the four-leech and similar ones, and it is advisable for you to loosen the bandages.
Examine the wound every day at both dusk and dawn until it closes, and it is advisable to place the limb in a position that makes it easy for pus to drain from it. If the wound persists for many days without healing or the pus does not drain from it, know that there are small shards of bone.
It is necessary to probe the wound with a probe; if it contains any foreign bodies, remove them and extract them. If part of it is not free, and it causes swelling or pain, cut it in pieces and remove it entirely. You can do this from all sides.
If the wound is exposed to dirt, ulcers or any other form of corruption and necrosis, it is necessary to treat each aspect of it with the appropriate remedy as described in their respective chapters.
It is important to remain with the patient and prepare mentally if a large bone is broken, such as the femur or humerus, which is one of the larger bones. Do not attempt to draw it out or extract it. Many times, death is averted by leaving it [the bone] alone; however, it may not heal for twenty or thirty days.
Treat the wound if you find a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter Ten.
Treatment of the swelling that occurs due to some fractures.
This swelling often occurs due to the inflammation caused by fractures, and it is rarely far from the joints; it causes the shape of the limb to deform and prevents the natural function of the limb.
If the tumor is soft, use medicines that coagulate such as scab, lime, lime-juice, antimony and similar things; take some of them or all of them, knead with a liquid that coagulates like vinegar, white of egg or oil.
Apply them on the tumor in a poultice and press it strongly upon it, leave the pressure for many days until it dissolves; then let it go and repeat the process until the tumor disappears, if God wills, or press a firm piece of lead on it.
The lead has a special property that dissolves everything in the body; if the tumor is hardened and becomes difficult to remove, break it from above and cut off the surrounding flesh or cauterize it with a cautery until it dissolves and heals the wound until it closes up.
Chapter Twenty and Ten.
In the treatment of fractures, if the bone breaks and remains in its original shape.
If a bone fracture occurs and the limb remains weak after that, it is because of several reasons. One of them might be due to excessive tying of ligatures on something other than what is appropriate, or it might be due to overtightening the ligatures until food cannot reach the organ, or it might be due to excessive and disorderly tugging, or it might be due to movements at an inappropriate time, or it might be due to a lack of blood in the body of the patient and weakness.
The treatment for this is feeding the patient and stimulating his body to increase blood in it, followed by the use of leeches and insertion of cupping glasses on him, along with moxibustion and similar methods, then placing a poultice on the organ to attract food to it or to relieve the tugging with warm water. until food flows through it and returns to its natural form.

This operation is very dangerous and can lead to a severe wound, but if the incision and suturing are made in a straight line, it should be washed with boiling water containing healing herbs such as comfrey leaves, onion roots, garlic, and similar things.

To relieve pain caused by swelling, use a smooth, thin needle that is always kept in the hand and move the limb to all sides at all times. If the swelling has worsened and become hardened and requires treatment with iron.

Fourth and Tenth Sections.
Treatment of the Lower Jaw Fracture.

To the throat, until it reaches the base of the skull, so that it may soothe the pain of the patient and he cannot grasp it, nor can it be compressed; it vibrates against his tongue with speech. Chapter 6 and 10.
Treatment of protrusion and hernia of the navel.
Know that the navel protrudes in three directions: one is that it protrudes towards the loin below, the second is that it protrudes towards the chest and possibly protrudes above the navel, and this occurs in the inguinal region.
And it does not protrude towards the back near the armpit or towards the front near the abdomen, and most often it protrudes and comes out below towards the inguinal region, especially in those with less subcutaneous tissue as it comes out quickly and returns quickly.
Those with many fleshy growths, it means that it (the growth) comes out in the yawning and enters in the yawning, and perhaps presents a blow or fall may cause the limb to twitch and the pain to be intense; he thinks that it has been broken. Chapter Seven and Forty.
Treatment of fractured mandible.
A fractured mandible heals slowly and also returns slowly, it extends in all directions and it is not hidden from you since it occurs under the sight and touch in any shape or form of a crack.
If the fractured mandible heals incorrectly, it will be apparent to you by the swelling of the fracture and the inability for it to move or for you to touch it without pain. It is important that you act quickly and reposition the mandible before it becomes exposed to cold or heat.
If it is exposed to cold or heat, repositioning it will be difficult and in most cases it may not heal well, especially if the mandible is behind. In such a case, all types of fractures are painful and death often accompanies them.
If it is something that can return, the attendant's hand should be forced back with both his hands and his arm extended. The physician should press from above and below until the fracture returns to its place.
If it is a dislocated jaw, it can be reduced by applying pressure with the hand until it reaches the concave part of the jawbone which it impinges upon. If the reduction does not occur, apply strong traction on both arms of the attendant.
The attendant must also be held so that he does not slip during the traction, then the arm is moved in all directions after pressing on his hand with a rolled cloth or wide thread. If the physician touches the fracture with his hand, it should be smeared with oil to facilitate the reduction of the fracture easily.
The fracture should be reduced forcefully until it returns, and after its return, a cast made of plaster with the setting of white lime should be applied tightly and left for several days. The arm should be tied from the neck of the attendant and left to rest.
Then, let it heal if the incision remains in its place. If you find that the suture has not become inflamed, undo and replace the sutures as needed, leaving him for some days until it becomes inflamed, then heal it. If something similar happens after the removal of the suture and it is healed again.
If it is difficult in movement, do not use the stretching in the bath or light massage and plucking until it softens, or put oil on the incision then suture it and leave for a day and night, then remove it and enter him into the bath. If swelling occurs, stretch the incision with a moderate tourniquet.
Then make oil applications on him three or four times with bathing until it softens. If you wish, apply warm goat fat mixed with oil and press it several times, for it will soften and return to its original state.
The eighth and tenth section.
On the treatment of a dislocation.
A dislocation of the hand often occurs and returns spontaneously, but it is necessary to hasten its reduction within the hour in which it occurred before setting or exposing it to heat.
Place the probe of the scapula on a flat surface and let the servant hold it. The physician shall place his hand on the division (between two bones) and push it until it returns, unless it is necessary to ensure that the fracture has gone deep into the wrist of the hand.
Place the probe of the scapula on a flat surface and let the servant hold it. The physician shall place his hand palm-down on the same division, for if it returns then the fracture is at the surface of the hand. If not, he should push gently to reduce the dislocation.
Leave it for a while and it will not return, except that the fracture remains in its place and does no harm to the probe, unless the hand has become stiff and cannot be grasped.
If it is certain that the nerve has been cut or severed, then there is no solution except to put pressure on the area (with a bandage), for perhaps it may help, but perhaps not, so if the probe returns, hold it with the bandage mentioned earlier and apply pressure for five days.
Then it heals and the hand becomes rigid with something in it, so immerse it in hot water and massage it repeatedly until it softens.
The Ninth and Tenth Chapters.
Treatise on the Dislocation of Fingers.
Treatment of Dislocation of Fingers: When a finger gets dislocated either to the outer or inner side of the hand, measure its length and straighten it with your forceps until it returns to its place. Then, tie the end of the finger and suture it towards the direction it was dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release and extend it until it settles on its own on the third day. If it is at night, tie it according to the same procedure, but do not keep it bound during the day. Let it be free during the day and exercise it with movement, then bind it again at night. Repeat this process for several days until it heals completely.
And the same treatment applies if it gets dislocated towards the inside of the hand, tie it in the direction of the same side and perform the same procedure as before until it returns to its place. Similarly, do the same when it gets dislocated to any other direction.
The Thirtieth Chapter.

Then tie it to prevent recurrence of the dislocation.
When a shoulder joint or neck is dislocated and the dislocation cannot be reduced, there is no treatment since death is imminent.

On a raised platform near the wall, spread something underneath it and moisten it so as not to hurt his chest, then place an upright piece of wood in a hole in the ground next to the platform towards his head and another piece of wood towards his legs on the other side of the platform.

The thirty-first chapter.
In the treatment of the hernia that is protruding.
Know that the ligament of the hernia and the ligament of the inguinal canal only expose to the bite, and neither of them exposes to all types of swelling and sinking. The ligament of the hernia protrudes in four directions.
That is because it protrudes towards the inside and outside and towards the front and back, and it mainly protrudes towards the inside and less so towards the front or the back. A sign of protrusion towards the inside is that if you press the correct hand of the patient on the hernia, it will be longer.

The sign that the bite exposes to the outside is characterized by symptoms opposite to these symptoms, and the sign that the bite exposes to the front is that it spreads the leg completely, except that it does not separate it without pain being present in the hernia, and even if he walks, he cannot do so towards the front.
The bladder is strained and the stone is pushed towards the posterior part of the urethra. A sign that the stone reaches the bladder neck is that it cannot spread the back and cannot be crushed before the bladder is first crushed, and the urine stream will be shorter than usual.

Unless [the operation] is on the side of health, place the blade and bind it in the manner as we have described in the Hadabah (a type of surgical ligature), except that it is desirable for the patient to be anesthetized on the affected side. If the incision is made in front, then the patient's limb should be stretched by pulling it once, and this description applies to the surgeon looking directly at the wound.
The surgeon places his left hand on the affected artery, then presses it with the other hand while exerting a continuous pressure that moves downwards towards the heel (of the foot). If the incision is made behind, stretching the limb downwards should not be done.
The patient should be placed on a firm surface, and it is desirable for him to be secured with bands at the ankle and waist as we have described in our previous discussion of ligatures, which applies to both the surgeon's left and right hands while performing the operation.
In the location where the severed artery has emerged, use a ligature to bind it, and if the severed vein returns, as indicated by the limb stretching, this should not be concealed from you; stretch the patient's limb.
If you see them [the fractured bones] level, and the upper one is pulled back without any force, then know that the limb has returned to its proper state. At this point, align the bones and apply a splint tightly so that the fracture does not move in any direction.
The upper bone should be immobilized for three or four days, then the splint and bandages are removed, and the limb is straightened with the other one. If they appear equal in position, know that the fracture has healed. Release the upper bone to walk. However, if you see any sign of deformation.
Return and immobilize it again, tighten the splint as before, and leave it for three days, then release it to walk on it for a few days until it strengthens, by God's will.
The second and third sections.
On the treatment of fractured bones.
A fracture occurs in three directions: it breaks inward, outward, and downwards, meaning towards the back, not toward the front of the limb. A sign of a fracture is when the upper bone requests that the lower one be grasped with its hand.
If it does not adhere to the bone, know that the fracture is dislocated, and apply traction on all sides until it sits down, and if it has strength to do so, let the patient sit on a chair with his affected leg hanging down. Then, have an assistant behind him to hold and pull it slightly towards the back. Then, you sit on the fractured leg.
Place your back to his face and make him stand between your legs, then hold onto his fractured leg with both hands, interlocking your fingers around it. Then, pull the fracture towards your side with strength, while another assistant pushes his leg until the fracture returns to its original position.
If it returns to its place smoothly, tie it and let the bone attach to the leg. Then, bind them all with a splint for three or four days, then release them. Do not walk on it except a little until it gets stronger.
If it is difficult for you to return it as described, use strong traction with the splints mentioned in the treatment of dislocations until it returns.
The third and thirtieth chapter.
In treatment of dislocation of the shoulder.
On the reduction of dislocated shoulder. The shoulder joint may dislocate easily and require complete reduction, which could be either back to its original position or out of it. A sign of successful reduction is that the shoulder appears swollen towards the direction to which it was dislocated.
As for the treatment of a dislocated shoulder, it is easy to reduce it by applying pressure with hands and gently manipulating until it returns to its original position. If the shoulder has been completely dislocated, the patient should sit up straight and hold onto a strong assistant standing behind him in the middle.
Then grab your right hand at the top of its foot and your left hand at the bottom of its foot in a standing position, then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand twice, then push it gently away from you with your left hand while pulling it backwards with your right hand without force.
Do this procedure twice as described, then push the heel towards the buttock in the third movement while you pull up on the ankle. If it returns at any point during these movements or within two movements like this and you find the foot level, continue with the same procedure. Otherwise, repeat the process until it does return, for it will eventually do so.
If you prevent its return as we have described, otherwise lay the patient on his back on the ground and tie him firmly to the earth so it happens between his legs where a wound has been made, but he does not feel it, then the attendant ties the leg.
Then the attendant moves the other leg, either with his hand or with a rope tying it around the neck of the patient, then each attendant pulls in opposite directions and the attendant holding the affected leg keeps it upright to prevent the body from moving down due to the pull, then the doctor straightens the fracture with his hand.
Then the other attendant holds the healthy leg down because the fracture will return quickly, if the fracture returns and it is clear to you that it has returned correctly, then take the bandages and the splints and tie them firmly with ropes and bind the foot with the rope towards the bottom. It is important to be cautious of the nerve that may be behind the heel.
Ensure that the rope is not too tight so as not to hurt him, then leave it for a few days or three. If the rope loosens, tighten it and then release it on the third or fourth day. The patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
If a fracture of the leg occurs before this period, it will not be secure enough for the tooth to affect it or spoil, and no treatment will be effective afterwards. If heated iron is applied to it (the fracture), it must be cooled down with what we have previously described as being used in a situation other than healing and traction until it subsides.
The fourth and thirty-first section.
On the treatment of a fracture in the fingers of the leg.
It is necessary to splint the fractured part of the fingers with a gentle material that is not a bone, and this is easy rather than difficult. If the fracture is in some parts of the palm of the foot, it is necessary for the patient to put his foot on an even place on the ground.
Or on a flat surface, and stand like you are walking, then place your foot on one of the splints, then grasp it with your foot and press it firmly until it returns to its original position without any movement being visible.
Then place a flat object under the sole of his foot to hold the entire foot firmly, then tighten it strongly and securely for three days, then release it and let him walk for many days until it recovers and the return is safe, God willing.
Chapter Fifty-Third.
Types of Fractures that Occur Alongside Injury or Break, or Both Together.
When such a thing occurs and treatment is attempted, it often ends in death.
Therefore, it should not be attempted to treat such cases except by a skilled person with long experience, sharp and precise eyes, no tremor, no trembling of the hands, no shaking, and he must use in the beginning only drugs that relieve acute inflammation, and leave it to its fate.
May Allah grant the ability, except for what I hope for safety from pain with a slight illness and you appear in it with some hope; formulate your response from your time before the inflammation occurs.
If the limb returns to its normal state, administer the treatment that relieves acute inflammation and heals injuries with suitable cauterization. If the fracture occurred in the bone with clean shards, extract them. This applies similarly to what we have discussed under simple diseases when dealing with their locations.
Exert your effort, beware of entering the path of destruction for what you are dealing with, and my advice to you is to leave it alone and submit to its fate, if Allah wills.
